		<shortDescription>Nowhere close to the power of the original, but as a standalone film this is okay</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As remakes go, this one of THE OMEN isn't half bad. It's well shot, with an attractive colour palette and good stunts to recommend it. Technical aspects are superior and a Macro Beltrami score adds to the experience. There are some quite horrific moments and some decent performances in the cast. Live Schreiber, an actor who I've never liked much, acquits himself well in the role of Robert Thorn; he's no Gregory Peck, but he makes a decent job of it. Julia Stiles is less assured in a somewhat bland turn as the put-upon mother, but the supporting cast make up for this. There's a raving Michael Gambon in a cameo role; a scary Mia Farrow, ageless and reminiscent of her role in ROSEMARY'S BABY as the creepy nanny, a scene-stealing Pete Postlethwaite as the mad priest, and best of all, a mannered and well-crafted turn from David Thewlis as the photographer played by David Warner in the original (Thewlis matches Warner's performance measure for measure).My main complaint, as with so many remakes, is that this one follows the original too closely. A couple of deaths are changed, but for the most part it's exact. Where's the fun in that? Why couldn't we have had a happy ending for a change? Nevertheless, this OMEN is a good film, and very much better than OMEN IV, the last sequel before this. Some of the deaths are inventive and pleasantly shocking, and the new decapitation doesn't disappoint. Sometimes things threaten to get a bit like FINAL DESTINATION but for the most part this rises above the rest as a good, old fashioned horror thriller.</fullReview>

		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>If It Ain't Broke, Don't Fix It!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A remake of the 1976 horror classic The Omen (1976), an American official realizes that his young son may literally be the devil incarnate.This film is a remake of the classic original from 1976, and it is a shameful and unnecessary remake. The original was scary, tense, and featured a well-written screenplay by David Seltzer, and was directed masterfully by Richard Donner. On this other hand, this film was directed by John Moore, who took Seltzer's screenplay and tried to add more action and directed it like he was putting more emphasis on the action, and we ended up with a film that lacks the thrills and the scares. Even worse, the film is technically a shot- for-shot remake, with only a few differences added in. Remaking a movie shot-for-shot is an idea that almost never works. Gus Van Sant tried that move when he did the 1998 remake of Psycho, and even he admitted that it was a bad move. Apparently, the ones who gave this movie the greenlight probably never heard that saying: "If It Ain't Broke, Don't Fix It!", as the original is a classic and didn't need a remake, but it got one, and we ended up with this mess.Liev Schreiber is the new Robert Thorn. He was the only thing person in the movie who seemed good in my opinion. Granted, he's not as good as Gregory Peck was in the original (well, who is), but he at least tried. On the other hand, Julie Stiles, who plays Lee Remick's role of Katharine Thorn was terrible. Lee Remick played Katharine to where we were sorry to see her go through all of the bull that she went through. Stiles however, had no such moments. She came off as very spoiled, complaining, and just overall, a nag, to the point to where we wouldn't feel sorry for her should something happen to her. And to add to that, she was too young, there should have been somebody at least in her late 30's or early 40's. Furthermore, she and Screiber are completely unconvincing as a couple, to the point where they almost cancelled each other out, talent wise.Mia Farrow plays the new Mrs. Baylock. While Farrow's heart is in the right place, she lacks the creepiness that Billie Whitelaw had in the original. The same goes with the new kid playing Damien, he just looks the part, he completely lacks the creepy factors that the kid in the original had. And like I mentioned earlier, John Moore's directing is terrible too, he puts too much emphasis on trying to make everything look flashy, which is definitely not something to do in a movie like this. Back in 1976, the original got a job well done without the technology to overuse special effects and flashiness. This film on the other hand, no...just no...And more, Jerry Goldsmith won an Oscar for his work on the original, which was very well deserved, as it was haunting and suspenseful. Marco Beltrami's score for this film doesn't have those, it comes off as rather boring and not suspenseful. All in all, this is a very shameful remake of a classic. This film doesn't induce scares or thrills, it induces groans, and even a couple of unintentionally laughs deepening on the mood. If you want a good horror film, you are much better off watching the original, as it is a far superior film with a superior cast, superior directing, and more. John Moore should have very well left alone what didn't need tweaking. So remember from this film: "If It Ain't Broke, Don't Fix It!"1/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2014-03-21</date>
		<username>The_Film_Cricket</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Reminds me of a neighbor kid . . .</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I generally detest remakes. I'm sorry, but if it ain't broke don't fix it, or if it was already broken there's no need to smack it around. I enjoyed Richard Donner's 1976 horror classic The Omen though I can't say that I am consumed by it. The movie was a nice little time-killer but hardly a classic. What surprises me about the 2006 remake is that it doesn't make mediocrity out of mediocrity but actually improves on the original material. This is a solid film told with mood and atmosphere and characters, not from a lot of digital effects nick-nacks or that annoying "RUNT!" noise on the soundtrack that makes up most horror films, though it does have one or two.Even with some shortcomings, I have to say I really enjoyed this film because it was true to the original story. It doesn't hammer us with a lot of needless visual effects but allows the situation to come out of real life. The movie begins where The Da Vinci Code fears to tread, with astronomers from the Vatican observing three shooting stars in the sky then interpreting them as a sign that The Son of Satan is born this night. That leads to an interesting but ill-advised lecture in which the signs in the book of Revelation point to the Tsunami in Indonesa, Hurricane Katrina and the attacks of September 11th. Those scenes integrated into an entertainment film took me out of the movie because I think there are other ways to get your point across. But anyway, let's move on.Meanwhile in Rome, a baby is born to an American Ambassador and then dies. A doctor quietly informs the father Robert Thorn (Liev Schreiber) that he can have the son of an unwed mother and that his wife need not know about it (I was waiting for the mother to try and figure out who the kid resembles but it never comes up). Our first clue as to Damien's troubles begin when his nanny hangs herself during his birthday party. It is a disturbing moment that, even in a remake, still works.Ominus signs bubble-up that suggest that Damien is not quite right. He's never sick, kids don't play with him, a snarling drooling rotweiler is always lurking about, zoo animals become violent in his presence, he nearly pulls his mother's hair out on his way to church and, oh yes, there's that business of his mother on the stairway balcony. It gets worse with the typical scenes of nervous priests babbling about books of the bible while trying to convince Thorn that "Your Son Must Die!!" His reaction is pretty much as a father would react, tell the nervous priest to take a hike until the bodies start stacking up then maybe consider hearing him out.After it becomes clear to Robert that his son is not quite human there is a long road trip in which he and a good-natured tabloid photographer named Jennings played by David Thewlis (who played Lupin in the third Harry Potter film) travel back to Rome to solve the mystery. The photographer is along to keep Robert on track because he's noticed that his photographs seem to portend death. A white line across his neck in one photo shows the he will die soon as well and anyone who has seen the original already knows his infamous fate.Those scenes in Rome are some of the best looking in the film, using reds and browns and light and shadow to suggest an eerie presence that is constantly shadowing them. There is a tense, very quiet scene that had me leaning forward with fascination, as Thorn and the photographer visit a half-dead priest (who could take skin-care tips from Emperor Palpatine) and ask for information. The man barely communicates but the scene proceeds almost in chilling whispers.A film like this needs an anchor and like Linda Blair who played the center of The Exorcist while still maintaining a supporting role this one includes a chillingly effective performance by little Seamus Fitzpatrick who doesn't mug but simply observes, squints, grins and looks up at his grown-up parents. He has exactly two words of dialog and for my taste that's probably more than the movie needs. His screen time gets smaller and smaller as the films progresses but what he leaves us with in the early scenes is chillingly effective.The most valuable thing that The Omen has to offer is that it isn't a splatter movie with a lot of dumbed-down scenes with things jumping out of the sides of the screen, the movie considers the situations and is more interested in displaying a tone, a mood that comes naturally from the story. There are long passages in the film with little to no dialog and when there is an action scene it's brief and to the point. I mentioned The Exorcist and this film reminds me of some of the qualities I valued from that film.There is a realistic setting with an unrealistic motivation at it's core and having grounded us in a narrative that we are familiar with, that makes the shocks resonate more. There are many deaths in this movie but the movie works it's way toward them. There are the usual impalings, be-headings, shootings, burnings but it's not used as splatter porn but as a means of reminding us what we're dealing with here.Thirty years after the original Omen, this movie stands on his it's own. It's almost shot for shot but it avoids the copy-cat catastrophe of that awful Psycho remake. Not much is tinkered with in the story department but I think the filmmakers have given us a better and scarier experience.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I should start off by noting that I never saw the original, so I'm reviewing this film completely objectively. With that said, this is just another one of those overdone modern horror movies that are so poorly executed and contain such dry acting that you are cheering for the character's death the whole way through.The plot is a strong one. Some of the best horrors out there - The Exorcist in mind - have strong religious overtones. And no one can deny how creepy a scary child villain is. The concept of an evil son is really riveting throughout the whole movie and pushes the boundaries of 'family before all else'. As Damien grew worse and worse throughout the movie, I kept asking myself if I could really take a child's life under such circumstances. Would we really have the willpower?Unfortunately, a good plot instantly dies with bad characters and acting. The kid and photographer are the only good, believable characters. The priest is just ridiculous. If you are trying to convince someone that their son is going to cause Armageddon, perhaps use less religious fear-inducing rambling and a little more logical English. He might have been a bit crazy, but no one is that stupid. But what really lost it for me is the terrible character of Robert Thorn, the great Ambassador and the even worse actor who plays him. I absolutely lost it during the car scene where Damien savagely attacks his mother. There is no possible way that a man of his intelligence would dismiss it as easily as he did. Furthermore, he chooses to smash through the gate when he could have easily told the guards that he needed to take his son to a doctor. None of this is helped by Schreiber's impossibly boring acting. When he should be crying, he has a straight face. When he should be laughing, he has a straight face. No matter how professional his character's role was, he went through an emotional roller-coaster with one ridiculous expression.Otherwise, the film has a couple of neat death scenes and frightening moments coupled with impressive cinematography in the house. It obviously has a strong plot with a lot of potential. 		<shortDescription>Cheap jump scares aside it's intelligent and creepy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Remakes of good films are always unnecessary, remake the bad ones. Never the less, notable horrors The Texas Chainsaw Massacre (2003) Dawn of the Dead (2004), and The Amityville Horror (2005) success paved the way for remakes of 'classic's'.The Omen (2006) is no exception, basically the same as it's 1976 predecessor there are a few tweaks to the story but the core plot remains the same, an American ambassador comes to terms with the fact his son is literally the Antichrist.It's a star studded cast including the likes of David Thewlis, Pete Postlethwaite, and Michael Gambon who give more than adequate supporting performances. Note worthy is Mia Farrow as the nanny, an elegant, intelligent and creepy Mrs. BaylockJulia Stiles is on form as the mother who senses something is wrong with her child and Liev Schreiber is excellent as the father Robert Thorn, who holds a secret about their son. It's fast paced and you see glimpse of Damien ageing from new-born via home videos. Arguably the child actor is not as menacing as the children in recent horrors but is effective enough, as most of the frivolities happen when he's not on screen.The film is superbly crafted by director John Moore, the locations and David Seltzer's well written screenplay give the film a realist weight. Even though there's some over saturated post-production lighting, the movie has a terrific ominous look, containing plenty of rain and shadows. Accompanied by Marco Beltrami's excellent music score remarkable creepy scenes include a visit to a monastery and cemetery, Thorn's meeting with Postlethwaite's priest and some eerie dream sequences.Cheap jump scares aside and without drawing comparisons to the original, The Omen is a very intriguing and entertaining horror thriller that is worth your time.</fullReview>

		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>The Omen</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When the infant son of an American diplomat Robert Thorn dies soon after birth, Thorn (Liev Schreiber) is offered a chance to replace the dead baby with another one born at the same time. He accepts the offer to spare the pain of his unknowing wife (Julia Stiles) but later comes to understand that the decision was a big mistake: the new boy, called Damien (Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick), is the spawn of the Devil himself and after a few years tragedies start occurring in and around the family. Thorn is driven to desperate acts to set things straight again...As anyone who has seen the original 1976 film can tell by reading the description above, the plot of the remake follows the original pretty faithfully. The main difference is that the events have been updated to the 21st century and some references to politics and current events are made in an attempt to make the rise of the Devil feel more relevant to modern audiences. Otherwise the two movies are very similar: both rely on atmosphere rather than gore and focus on the Robert Thorn character more than Damien. Even the infamous kill scenes of the original film are repeated almost identically, raising the question if there was any kind of point in remaking the effort. The new film is not completely useless, however.The most memorable thing about the remake is the cinematography. The tinting of the scenes varies greatly over the course of the story; ranging from the pale, natural lighting of the mother's dreams to the cold, blue atmosphere of Italy and the green, sickly air in the church during the finale, adding to the otherworldly feel of the locations. I also liked the addition of some surprising dream sequences which provide the only real scares in the film.One thing I didn't like as much are the actors. While Schreiber handles his role with relative ease, his charisma is no match to Gregory Peck of the first film and Julia Stiles comes across as somewhat unconvincing and bland as the mother. Young Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick doesn't get to say much in his reticent role but I felt that some of his facial expressions were a little too exaggerated to ring true. David Thewlis is OK as an investigating photographer though.I may have put too much emphasis on contrasting the remake with the original film but I feel the two movies are so similar that such comparisons are justified. If you have seen the 1976 film, there's not much to discover in the remake besides the colourful cinematography. As a film of its own, the remake is watchable if not very memorable; more suspense would have been needed to make it stand out among its peers. The remake is, however, better than the disappointing sequels of the original Omen.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Hollywood Rummages Through The Back Numbers Once More</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Mathematics demonstrate that as there are only a finite number of musical notes; one day every possible tune will have been composed. I guess a corresponding constraint must also apply to movies, and I have the impression from the number of sequels, prequels, and sundry clones, that Hollywood has more-or-less reached that point. Only last week I got to digest the remake of a much-admired original in 'Flight Of The Phoenix'. My comments can be found elsewhere; I wasn't impressed.This time it's the turn of 'The Omen'. I should say that I was never a big fan of the original though it was an excellently crafted and well-plotted chiller with a first-rate star in the form of Gregory Peck. Maybe it's because I've grown to be a devout atheist that religion-themed movies tend to leave me feeling a little ho-hum. There are just too many unanswered - and for the most part unanswerable - questions. You either take the thing at face value or leave it. Obviously; if you're in the habit of saying prayers every night then something in the convoluted gravitas is bound to appeal. But as I say; for me it's just hokum. Still; the original had lots of Gothic creepiness. It was well-acted with shocks-a-plenty. Some implausible encounters and coincidences that would have surprised even Charles Dickens had to be glossed-over if the spell of believability wasn't to be broken. Yet, in the main, it worked. Though I still think most of its success was owed to Gregory Peck and the powerful characterisation he provided. Well; here we are 30 years on and - like the 'Phoenix' - risen again. There's absolutely nothing new. The whole thing just plods after the first, frequently treading in its very footsteps. I even glimpsed the words 'Recycled Pooch' stamped on the rottweilers' bums. There's a much-improved and slicker multi-channel sound score. Some of the photography is more ambitious too. But otherwise it's business as usual, except that there are no top-dollar stars to prop it up. This time the cast list resembles a series of anonymous anagrams with the possible exception of Pete Postlthwaite, who scuttles in and out of the shadows as if he's still running numbers for Kyser Soze. A fast-fading Mia Farrow - who's already had her own infernal brat issues - makes a suitably creepy cameo. There's even one of those tiresome endless thunderstorms - probably got cheap from a Hammer Studios fire-sale.You know it's often said that the Lord moves in mysterious ways, but does the Devil have to do the same? You'd have though he'd have worked his ticket to the contrary and been as straightforward and direct as George Spigott ('Bedazzled'). Why, for example, go to the trouble of having a builder kick his hammer so that it slips down the tiles of a roof, falls onto a corroded cafe sign, breaking the bracket off and allowing the whole gubbins to swing through 180 degrees, just in time to catch the intended victim stand-up and be decapitated. I mean - come on. You're 'avin' a laugh ain'cha? Why not just give the geezer a heart-attack? The Horned One at least has a genuine sense of humour. He'd also make an excellent snooker-player. Then again; neither God nor the Devil are a match for old Death. Just check out the tricks he gets up to in the 'Final Destination' franchise. Death: there's just no living with him.The movie is adequate as it stands (if you like this sort of thing) but 30 years of polish haven't made it shine any brighter.</fullReview>

Since the original 1976 version is a good movie, this one was decent as well. However, like most remakes, the director managed to remove the subtleties that made the original movie great. In the original Omen the director left uncertainty as to if Damien was really the spawn of Satan. However, keeping with the stile of most movies made in the last 20 years, the remake assumed the audience was composed of low-grade morons and removed any such ambiguity.I'll provide one example to demonstrate my point. Both movies contain a scene where Damien goes to a zoo and all the animals go crazy in his presence. In the original 1976 movie Damien looks innocent and even frightened by this. However, in the 2006 remake Damien has an evil look to him (something he has the entire movie with a touch of dark red makeup around his eyes) as he heartlessly proclaims, "They fear me." Subtle differences, but drastic in changing the movie's tone.In summary, this isn't a bad movie and stays fairly true to the original. However, if you have the option of choosing between the two? Choose the original 1976 movie. It truly is better.---That being said, why remake a perfectly fine move? I'll never know. It's not like the original "Omen" had any real need to be remade. I guess I shouldn't be surprised. I mean, it's not like Hollywood isn't constantly re-using old plots anyway. At least the movie studio didn't insult our intelligence by calling this movie by a different name like "666: the Child" (a blatant "Omen" rip-off that came out the same year as this remake).</fullReview>

		<username>ta_naemhni</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Misses the point of a "remake".</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I rented the original and the remake at the same time specifically for comparison purposes. The original was good (of course), and the remake is reasonably serviceable, but that's largely because the remake is a remake in the literal sense of the term.What the filmmakers didn't "get" here is that the term "remake" is something of a misnomer. The point of a "remake" isn't actually to make the film again, but to re-imagine it. "The Omen" is a remake in that, as others have pointed out, it follows the original film so closely that it actually does make the film again, the differences from the original being essentially inconsequential (e.g., in Kate Thorn's falling scene, Damien rides a scooter instead of a tricycle, and the falling object is a potted plant instead of a fishbowl). Even at that, some of the changes don't work -- compare the original Damien Thorn's attitude and behavior toward the dog in the house to the remade Damien Thorn's, for example, and the complete elimination of Father Brennan's death scene in the remake.In addition, the casting choices are a bit iffy. Liev Schreiber and Julia Stiles are not bad actors, but casting younger actors in the roles of the Thorns doesn't work as well as Gregory Peck and Lee Remick did. That is, of course, largely because of changing business demands by Hollywood, but it still detracts from the effectiveness of the film. A more mature male lead, in particular, would have made the film much more effective, as Peck did in the original. Pete Postlethwaite, for example, an excellent though underrated actor, would probably have been better cast as Robert Thorn rather than in the minor role of Father Brennan.I'm not sorry I watched the film, because it was actually very educational for me in learning more about film criticism. I just wish that the lessons I learned had been more positive rather than negative.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>THE OMEN: A More Humanistic Approach</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Remembering the 1976 new film THE OMEN this viewer recalls someone making a dog growling sound in the parking lot at movie's end and how that effect sent gasps and chills through those who had just witnessed Damien and his dogs - a sign of a thoroughly successful thriller. Now thirty years later (and having the original effect watered down by the subsequent three sequels) out comes a remake of THE OMEN and once again the story and production values of the film jolt even the more staid viewers. Armageddon now seems frightfully at hand! Though the script is essentially intact form the original version, this OMEN is cast with actors who make the story more credible because they imbue it with more humanly drawn character interpretations. Much of the power of the film lies in the superb performance by Liev Schreiber as the politically important father of a 'stillborn' son that was 'exchanged' at birth: he gives a warmth and honesty that were elusive in Gregory Peck's performance. Much the same can be said about Pete Postlethwaite's tormented priest who tries to alter the events threatened if the 'adopted' strange son Damien is allowed to mature, and to David Thewlis as the photographer who helps Schreiber's character come to grips with the decision he made about child swapping on the day of the birth of Damien. Mia Farrow, cosmetically enhanced to look oddly young, is the smarmy nanny and Michael Chabon gives a bit of class to the priest with the dreaded secrets on how to end the impending destruction if Damien is allowed to live. The weak link in the cast is Julia Stiles who here just can't seem to inhabit the role of the increasingly terrified mother of Damien: likewise the child selected to portray Damien (Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick) appears more a makeup artist's vision rather than a child whose apparent normalcy could have made the evil within more terrifying.The special effects are used well as is a musical score by Marco Beltrami (though not nearly as memorable as that by Jerry Goldsmith in the original). An interesting finding is that the original 6-year-old actor portraying Damien (Harvey Stephens) is cast in a tiny part here as a tabloid reporter, giving the feeling that the spirit of Damien is still pervasive. Director John Moore gets most of right. Not a bad movie as many have stated and one that certainly could have been more powerful with some casing changes, but this film still causes the jitters in an otherwise tranquil evening! Grady Harp</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Haven't we been here before?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The question is ? why? Why remake a superb movie that hasn't dated at all in the last thirty years? And if you must remake it, why do it as a scene-for-scene carbon copy of the original? It's quite a surreal experience, watching this. It really is the same film that you saw back in 1976. It's almost the same dialogue, the same camera angles. There are no surprises at all, even for the new generation who probably weren't around when the first version was made but no doubt are well aware of it due to its lasting appeal and impact.That's why I'm forced to mark this one down.It's undoubtedly well made. It looks good, John Moore directs with style, Liev Scheiber and Julia Stiles provide younger but equally weighty leads to Gregory Peck and Lee Remick, Marco Beltrami's score is excellent though not as good as the original (how could it be?). But it's nothing more than a re-tread. At least the new versions of The Amityville Horror and The Wicker Man ? appalling though they were ? attempted to divert from the original story lines. This doesn't give us anything new at all. Even the death sequences are duplicates of the first ones ? which left me feeling particularly short-changed. Surely they could have updated the gore at little? The lesser characters, though well cast, are no improvement on their forerunners. Mia Farrow is devilishly cunning as the starched Mrs. Baylock, but back in '76 Billie Whitelaw didn't have the 'demon child' baggage that Farrow has, and thus worked better. As ill-fated photographer Jennings, David Thewliss is fine and loses his head more spectacularly than David Warner did, but that's the most you can say for him. Pete Postlethwaite is Father Brennan ? an actor cut very much from the same angst-ridden cloth as Pat Troughton, who did it first. Again, he's no worse but no better. Which is more than can be said for Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick in the key role of Damien. He's certainly more sinister than Harvey Stephens was in 1976, but there's the rub: Stephens came over as nothing more than a spoilt, irritating brat. And wouldn't that be the way the Anti-Christ would appear in his infant years? The modern Damien, though underplayed (rather nicely, it has to be said) by Davey-Fitzpatrick, is intelligent and evil way beyond his years, and for this reason seems less real.The only moment when this version comes even close to improving on the earlier one, is in the scene at the remote monastery in Italy. It's particularly spooky, but only serves to irritate more because it shows what the film-makers could have done with this story if they'd been prepared to attempt a completely new interpretation and perhaps had veered even further into the realms of full-blown horror.Hollywood's lack of adventurousness when it comes to big-money chillers is something that should dismay all fans of the genre. There was so much money, effort and talent put into this ? and for what?</fullReview>

It's hard to get past things such as these and it does tend to get annoyed at these basic inaccuracies.I'd say that if Omen had been made in 2006 as it's own film it would of worked but when compared to the original (and you can't help but compare) this fades into oblivion. 		<username>Christopher T. Chase (cchase@onebox.com)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>The "Devil" You Know...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Just when a film like Alex Aja's THE HILLS HAVE EYES was starting to restore my faith in remakes again, along comes a movie like THE OMEN to screw it all up and prove yet again, that there really is no reason to remake a classic if you have nothing new to bring to the table.Aside from the benefits of updated technology, resulting in crisper, clearer photography and somewhat improved F/X staging, this version sadly lacks any of the scary sharpness of the original. And the very first evidence of this is in the score. Marco Beltrami is a really good composer, but c'mon! Jerry Goldsmith scored an Oscar for his contributions to the original, and with good reason. At least 85% of why audiences were wetting themselves over Damien back in the Seventies was because of that soundtrack. Any of the scenes here which slavishly reproduce parts of the original, fall with a loud 'THUD' when the new music provides none of the original's "juice." The second aspect of why this doesn't work is the casting. Liev Schreiber and Julia Stiles? Good actors, yes. Will they make you forget Gregory Peck and Lee Remick? Not likely. Even the supporting cast here winds up portraying pale imitations of the original characters.Especially blatant is Michael Gambon appearing as a surrogate for the late, great Leo McKern. Gambon isn't even afforded the screen time required to make an impression as the religious archaeologist Carl Bugenhagen. David Thewlis and Pete Postlethwaite doubling for David Warner and Patrick Troughton respectively, pretty much suffer the same fate. Only Mia Farrow as the demented Mrs. Baylock comes close to equaling the scariness of the memorable Billie Whitelaw. And Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick makes a spooky stand-in for Harvey Stephens.The cynical, contemptuous effort to goose new life into an old franchise is the only reason why this movie was made...as yet another potential "cash cow." With no offense to the cast or crew, my advice to lovers of the original film? Stick with "the devil you know"...Having viewed this on HBO, I was so glad that I skipped this mess in theaters. But I still would like a refund of my two hours...well, the parts of that time I didn't find myself dozing off, that is...</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Heed the Review</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Five years ago senior American diplomat Robert Thorne's (Liev Schreiber) wife Katherine (Julia Stiles) had a delivery that resulted in the death of their newborn child. The thing is, she doesn't know anything about it. The hospital priest, Father Spiletto (Giovanni Lambardo Radice) convinced Robert to take and raise another child born that same night whose mother died during delivery. The child's name, Damien. (Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick) Years pass and Damien is happy as well as healthy. Everything appears perfect. That is until now, beginning at his birthday party where his nanny commits suicide. The occurrences continue with warnings from a strange priest, Father Brennan (Pete Poslethwaite) claiming Robert must defeat the son of the Devil. As the story progresses and more out of the normal events take place, Robert slowly begins to piece together the puzzle and realize the horrid truth. Damien is no ordinary child, he is none other than the Anti-Christ. It is then he learns that in order to stop the dreadful catastrophe yet to occur he must kill his own son. Or so who he believed to be his own.Sounds interesting doesn't it? Perhaps it might be if the exact story hadn't been told in theaters thirty years ago. There lies one of the problems with this extremely unnecessary remake of 'The Omen'. It is so similar in almost every way it's disappointingly scary. Aside from a few death scenes and an added sixteen second dream sequence, the only real difference here is the characters have cell phones, digital cameras, &amp; the internet.The other problem, the acting. It was so close to grisly it just barely slides by as mediocre. Julia Stiles certainly did not amaze by any means. It isn't until shortly before we last see her that she almost manages to get a hold of her character and begin to come through. Possibly her next role as Glenna in 'Edmond' being released next week in select theaters will suit her better and give her a chance to shine.I really wanted this remake to surpass its predetermined God-awfulness, but it didn't. Director John Moore tried to go a good job here but was unable to succeed. What he couldn't seem to grasp is when you re-make a film, you're supposed to bring and present the same feeling and likeness the initial movie contained, while still making it your own and adding your own print to it. Not rehash the same one and wrap it in a new package. He wanted to bring back a classic horror film and make it great all over again, but it didn't just happen. The fact it's practically scene for scene the same as the original is mindlessly idiotic. Heed my words, see the original 1976 'Omen', not this lousy remake. In it you'll find far better acting and an overall more enjoyable viewing experience.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Beats the Devil</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have very strong opinions about remakes - all overridden by this one key principle; if you are going to do a remake - do it because something in the original was not done too well or lacked technology to tell its story convincingly or because in it you can remake it keeping its heart while making necessary creative differences that will greatly enhance the original. None of these apply to this version of The Omen. Don't get me wrong. It is not a bad film as films go, just incredibly needless when compared with its much superior original. Liev Schrieber does an adequate job as Robert Thorn - a man that quickly realizes he is the mortal father of the spawn of Satan. His wife is anemically played by Julia Stiles with virtually nothing to do. Mia Farrow turns in the best performance as the wicked nanny. Nice to see her acting again. All the classic scenes are done here again - the church scene seems to be almost the same frame by frame except shows much more blood. That was one obvious difference. Director John Moore does show much more violence. The movie is a quality film in terms of the director's ability, the general acting talent, the beautiful sets and locations(perhaps more impressive here than in the original), and slicker production values. What's missing then? Why was it made? One thing that immediately comes to mind for NOT being in the film is the original's haunting score. It was sorely missed here. Maybe, some of the great Hollywood minds will stop making unnecessary remakes of films that are barely 30 years old and make remakes, if that has to be, of movies that weren't done all too well in the first place. Unfortunately for The Omen, the first one is hard to beat.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Great Cinematography and Decent Enough Acting Combined With an Intriguing Storyline Make...The Omen</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Late one night in the Vatican Observatory, a young priest spots a large comet flaring across the night sky. He quickly consults his father (preist), who confirms that this comet is the latest in a series of the fore signs of the Apocalypse.Back in Rome, deputy ambassador Robert Thorn is rushing to the hospital for the birth of his child. 'Apparently there are complications,' he says. When he arrives, he finds the baby has died. Heartbroken, he seeks to spare his wife the pain of the bad news, and secretly adopts another child, born at the same time. The baby has a cute red bed as well.Unbeknownst to Robert or his wife, this child is the Antichrist.After a series of grotesque and suspicious deaths, Robert begins to realize the truth--and seeks to destroy the evil.As with any John Moore picture, this movie is just marvelous to look at. The cinematography is superbly dark and blue, conveying the gloom sense of doom (alliteration intended). Jonathan Sela's use of dramatic lighting and smooth camera movement is worth giving this movie look--even if you don't like it very much.The performances by the actors were decent enough. The three male leads were the best in the film, although this isn't saying much, considering that we have Liev Schreiber, David Thewlis, and Pete Postelwaithe (sp?). The only irk I have with any actor in the film is Julia Stiles. She is a very pretty woman, and perhaps the best choice for Cate Thorn, but her acting is rather wooden. But she still passes in my book.The music by Marco Beltrami is okay in some parts, but ultimately falls into the category of 'jump scare score'. That being said, this movie is not very scary at all. It is exciting and in parts, surprising. The last ten minutes of the film are heart-throbbing, but if you've seen the original, you probably won't be surprised at all.Overall, this is one of the better remakes I've seen. It has its flaws, but it is worthy to enter my DVD collection.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Another Plagued John Moore Thriller</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Granted, I have only seen two John Moore films before watching "The Omen." And those two were "Behind Enemy Lines", and "Flight of the Phoenix." Both of these films are painfully terrible, but the problems of both films seem to have nothing to do with the director. In Behind Enemy Lines, Mr. Moore's effort was notable, but nothing could help the movie. The script, acting, and basic idea for the film were absent. The same can be said for Flight of the Phoenix. Honestly, the only thing that makes Flight watchable is the fact that Dr. House stops by for a few scenes. With "The Omen" Mr. Moore seems to have adopted a new visual style, one that's simple, dark, and haunting. Is this even the same John Moore? Because of less-than-perfect acting and plot, however, Mr. Moore is plagued again. Julia Stiles and Liev Schreiber play the wife of, and a U.S diplomat, respectively. The film opens in Italy, where Mr. Schreiber's character is the U.S Ambassador. He is walking briskly into a hospital, where his wife, Ms. Stiles, is giving birth. A doctor tells him that the baby died and that she is resting, but alive. Unbeknownst to his wife, Robert (Mr. Schreiber), swaps the dead baby for a living one, who needs parents. This, as it turns out, was not such a great idea. With a baby boy, Damian, added to the troupe, the family Thorn moves to England, where Robert is the new U.S Ambassador. Everything goes swimmingly until, on Damian's fifth birthday, his nanny kills herself by hanging off of the roof of their massive mansion. Damian seems relatively pleased with the event, or, at least, un-scarred. Soon after, a new nanny comes to visit (Mrs. Baylock, played by Mia Farrow.) She makes herself right at home, and soon invites a large black dog into the house, and engages in other mysterious doings. And then the movie really gets crazy: when the Thorns attempt to enter a church for the nanny's funeral service, Damien beings screaming, and rips a bloody hole in his mother's forehead. This is when they begin to suspect something is not quite right. All of this would be acceptable, but another element is added to the mix by the screenwriter, David Seltzer. A bunch of Italian high priests gather together with the Pope, and analyze how they know the world is going to end. They take a passage of the Bible, and apply it to the recent tragedies around the world: 9/11, Hurricane Katrina, and the Tsunami on Christmas 2005. This gives the film an angry, sadistic tone that should be saved for Damien and his father. The rest of the film is then clouded in confusion, bad writing and acting, and, most importantly, not nearly enough blood.</fullReview>

But recreating a classic virtually scene for scene, minus the haunting soundtrack of the original just seems kind of pointless.And yet the things they did change didn't add to it. The zoo scene here was not a patch on the "attack of the baboons" in the original.Under-30s who haven't seen the original may well enjoy this, but for the rest of us... why bother?</fullReview>

		<username>Robert_Hearth</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>"From The Eternal Sea He Rises?" --- Father Brennan (Pete Postlethwaite)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Omen" (2006)Directed By: John MooreStarring: Liev Schreiber, Julia Stiles, Mia Farrow, David Thewlis, Pete Postlethwaite, &amp; Seamus Davey-FitzpatrickMPAA Rating: "R" (for disturbing violent content, graphic images and some language)"From the eternal sea he rises, creating armies on either shore, turning man against his brother, until man exists no more."Question: What is the reasoning behind making a shot-by-shot remake of a horror classic? If you said "easy money", then, congratulations, Hollywood has not yet fooled you with its lame excuses, such as "We are introducing a horror classic to an all-new generation". I usually do not have problems with remakes. After all, a remake does not affect the quality of the original (unlike a mediocre sequel) and I do not have a problem with this film as a whole. I have a problem with the reasoning behinds its very existence. This movie was made for money but, as much as I hate to do so, I have to recommend it. "The Omen" (2006) is almost the exact same film that was made in 1976 except with CGI-enhanced scenes, more blood/gore, and a few key scenes altered almost completely (no doubt done to surprise fans of the original). In fact, it is so close to the original that David Seltzer is credited with penning the script. He did not. He wrote the original screenplay and had very little (if any) affiliation with this remake. I recommend it because, as unoriginal as it is, "The Omen" (2006) showcases a few intriguing new (and alternate) scenes and has such a beauty about it. This is a very classy horror movie and it seems to want to generally scare its audience, rather than simply gross them out.Do I even need to go over the plot with you? While most of you know how this movie begins and ends and know the plot that strings these two together, I will still explain for those who have never met little Damien Thorn before. Robert Thorn (Schreiber) is an American official who agrees to adopt a child when his wife, Katherine (Stiles), loses hers in childbirth. He keeps the adoption a secret from his young, unassuming wife and, for the first few years of their son's life, everything seems to be going perfectly. That is until Damien's nanny commits suicide at his sixth birthday party and everything goes downhill quickly. A mysterious priest (Postlethwaite) warns Robert who fails to heed the warning--that his son is actually the Antichrist. As the people who try to help Robert and Katherine begin to die in gruesome, but seemingly accidental ways, Robert realizes that he must end his own son's life?if he is to save the world from eternal hell. Father Brennan: I was at the hospital, Mr. Thorn, the night your son was born. Robert Thorn: What do you know about my son? Father Brennan: Everything.When "The Omen" (2006) ended, I realized that it was most effective as a companion to the original--a textbook that can be used to compare the difference between films today and those of thirty years ago. You cannot watch this movie expecting anything more. However, it does stand up by itself. It is a well-made movie and, surprisingly, scary by today's standards. The changes made to the original are all fairly intriguing (although they are not nearly as good as those in the 1976 film). The blood and gore has been increased greatly (which is a testament to how our world is becoming blunted to violence). Overall, "The Omen" (2006) has one huge fault and that is that it is a remake. Had it been original, this would have been a very impressive horror film, obviously. But, I cannot help but to see this film as a child standing in the shadows of its parents?only this movie will never step out of that shadow and, to some degree, that is very unfortunate.Final Thought: Pointless, yes, but it somehow manages to warrant a watch.Overall Rating: 6/10 (B)</fullReview>
	</review>

Pete Postlethwaite was one of the few solid actors here, yet even he was unable to find any fluidity in this role. And as the camera enters what should have been one of the most disturbing moments of the film -- Father Brennan's isolated dwelling ? we are met with a scene that teeters between utter failure and over-kill. The most authentic acting came from the dogs. If you've never seen the original Omen, I think you could actually be impressed with this film on some level. But if you want the hair on the back of your neck to stand up and to leave the theater with a new respect for evil, I recommend the original.</fullReview>

It felt like a very water downed version. None of the original score or a remix. Really, the score made much of the film. At least incorporate it. The black evil dog was a Shephard, which really dumbfounded me and then turned into a Rottweiler later. Okay?? Why? There were a few differences but nothing huge. There were more in-depth explanations of a few areas that the second and third film really shed light on, which I thought was smart. The scene where the priest dies wasn't as good as the original. I think the photographer's observances in this film weren't as interesting. In fact, if I had never seen the original I would've have been convinced of his ideas. It barely showed anything. With all the technology there could've been so many enhanced scenes and special effects. Another disappointment, the zoo scene.Some good points ... when Mr. Thorn runs over Mia Farrow's character. Very funny. The ending scene didn't drag out like a usual film would at the end, with typical overkill of suspense. The type where you think the bad guy is dead so you drop the weapon and then he comes back to life even though you shot his head off and his brains are crawling on the kitchen floor. He grabs a knife and attacks you again, then you REALLY kill him this time. No, this movie didn't bullshit with that. It did it's end. Thank you.Damien, I was disappointed in. Was the first one really so creepy or did he shape my ideas on what an evil boy would look like? The new Damien was like a pussy cat trying to give you a scowl. Yawn. No, he didn't scare me. The first one I knew mighty not to mess with. This one I would spank and send to bed without his Oreo cookies. He was indeed a very mock version. Especially when he overly and poorly imitated the original, sort of like American Idol. Anyway, there was some tiny added details to mix it up. No real change of anything however. No added intrigue. No alarms and no surprises. No real major highlights. Nothing that popped out and said HEY, this rocks. Nothing that really raised an eyebrow, save for the Mia scene. That was pretty amusing. So over all, I was disappointed but not enough to be angry. I chalk this one up to a film that didn't surpass it's original which is hard to do. Not impossible, but hard. I feel there was all the available means to make this film the best one possibly can, and didn't make use of it. We have 30 yrs? of technology and nothing could spin our hats around??I wouldn't give it a 5.5, that's a tad harsh, and I'm a rather harsh critic on films so I would gander around maybe a 7. It's worth the rent and the see for those of us who want more and more of The Omen and love every inch of whatever you can get. I even loved the girl child version ... so, with that said, watch it and enjoy it's reliving but don't expect any fireworks.</fullReview>

		<date>2006-09-25</date>
		<username>GildedPickle</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Remake hell</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I am beginning to tire somewhat of recent remakes of older movies/TV shows. Capitalisation upon (and subsequent embellishment of) earlier source material plays upon a sense of nostalgia for its advertisement, and the perverse logic of many studios runs "If movie A worked, movie B based upon the same premise/plot/characters MUST work, no matter how shabbily it is actually made: all one need do is throw money". Alas, this does not a good movie make.I had an early exposure to The Omen: I use the word "exposure" in the sense that I had crawled out of bed just in time to see some poor chap's head be severed from his torso by a sheet of glass on a truck, before I was whisked away to bed again by parents mindful of the tender age of their son.I had therefore come to expect some sensationalist and terrifying forms of death to be presented in the remake, and I wasn't wholly disappointed. Seeing Pete Postlethwaite being turned into a human kebab was entertaining (although I like this actor: perhaps Dale Winton would have been a better candidate...) Firstly, my reasons for affording the movie 5 stars: it was a film which engendered feelings of unease and unpredictability (apart from the obvious homages, e.g. the hanging nanny). There truly was a tense atmosphere throughout, although one scene in particular deserves mention. In the graveyard the ambassador finds the graves of his actual son, killed at childbirth by satanic doctors, and of Damien's mother, a jackal. Suffice to say that a skeleton baby is a rare thing to see in cinema, however there was also the feeling (not manifested) that perhaps it would twitch, attempt to talk, open eyes long since shut. That would probably have given me sleepless nights for a month or so.However several things let the film down: although the acting is not dire (for this see Miami Vice), it is somewhat substandard and portrayals of human emotion are occasionally stunted. Furthermore the aforementioned tension which has been present throughout is dispelled in the natural (and somewhat clichéd) horror manner: the background music stops for a few seconds, the camera is still, and suddenly one is then assaulted by a viciously loud noise and a rapid appearance of (supposedly) terrifying event or sight. It's unfortunate, but in the modern world where everyone has, at least once, been subjected to a flash screamer, or a "jumpy" horror movie, this sort of thing is easily anticipated. It is still not a pleasant experience, but the edge of terror is somewhat lost. I doubt that it is being done well: take Alien for example, perhaps the most effective use of this technique was demonstrated in the ventilation shaft, as the Alien appears briefly as light is shone upon it. Today's directors seem to have lost their methods of using it to full effect. Another solution (as used in The Skeleton Key) is to have the viewers expecting the fright or shock shot, only to continually deprive them of it and maintain the tension indefinitely.All in all a movie spoilt by the fact that it is a remake. Pity: it could have been good.</fullReview>
	</review>

All in all though, an excellent movie that deserves to be a hit but it will never assume the cult mantle it's forefather did.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A good attempt with some minor flaws</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>To be honest, I never cared much for the original Omen-films. I was intrigued by the basic idea behind it but after watching them they mostly left me behind with an unsatisfying feeling, it could have been better.I thought this remake with it's 6-6-6 release date was probably more a gimmick (easy free publicity) than a serious attempt to revive the Omen-series. This weekend I went out to see it anyway and was more or less pleasantly surprised when I left the cinema (thanks to my girlfriend as she insisted seeing this movie). The atmosphere of the movie is just right, it feels creepy and genuinely unpleasant to view this in a dark cinema. That kept me glued to my seat, accept for the few moments where I jumped out of it. This is one of the few movies that caught me completely off-guard with some of its shock elements and I consider that a very good thing. The acting isn't bad either although Liev Schreiber wouldn't have been my first pick for the male lead, he just seems to static and lacks charisma for this type of "loving father in distress"-roles.Is there a downside as well ? Oh yes there is. Plot holes....lots of plot holes, after about 30 minutes the movie shifts into a higher gear and logic gets thrown out of the window in order to wrap up the story in a reasonable time. Suddenly you see the main characters in situations where you, as a viewer, do not expect them to end up in the first place. It's such a big contrast, the first half of the movie is slow and dedicated to precisely telling the story whereas in the second part you feel that the director is standing just outside of view, telling the actors to hurry up. Also, for the gore, it seems that the script borrows a little too much from the Final Destination-series which sometimes seem to overrule the satanic main theme of the movie.Overall this movie is an enjoyable experience which I can recommend to anyone who has either seen or not seen the original. If you like suspense and don't care that images are more important than storyline you will have a great time. But....somewhere deep below....there's that unsatisfying feeling again.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The son of the devil turns up once more in this modern remake</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I heard they were remaking The Omen, I had a sense of disappointment. Firstly, the original film was perfectly okay, not brilliant but creepy enough by modern standards, secondly, there are few actors to match up to Gregory Peck's performance in the original. That all said, The Omen, remake, is not a bad film. Lieb Schreiber and Julia Stiles are just about believable as a modern, young American couple trying for a baby who finally end up with a creepy child that looks nothing like either of them and is actually the biblical Antichrist as told in the Book of Revelations who will bring about the end of the world. Some of the set pieces are quite nice. The journey to the monastery of Cervete is a wonderful, misty river Styx and hooded ferryman ride with a truly horrific priest/monk meeting with Thorne. There is also some good casting as well, such as that of David Thewlis as Jennings the photographer who helps Robert Thorne to understand what is going on with his son and why he is visited by mad priests and terrible accidents. Mia Farrow as the nanny Mrs Baylock is nowhere near as creepy as Billie Whitelaw, however. There was a genuine unsettling nastiness to Whitelaw's performance which Farrow doesn't quite capture, which is a pity because Farrow is a brilliant actress. In this film, she comes over as a bit madder/deranged but there is an unsettling edge of overprotection surrounding her relationship with Damien.For my money, though, Lieb does not come over quite as convincing in his portrayal as Robert Thorne, but when you've seen Peck's brilliantly, tired and worried performance in the original, its always going to be a hard one to compete with. Lieb does as well as he can but again, better casting might have helped. This is the sort of part that cries out for a Johnny Depp type actor, someone who could be utterly believable.A few things, however, do rankle about this film. One is Thewlis' photographer using chemical photography in the 21st century. Most press photographers now use digital cards for ease of transferring pictures to waiting editors. This could have so easily been updated to Thewlis examining his digital downloaded pictures on a computer screen and looking at the card in puzzlement because he thinks there's a fault with it. Somehow having developed photos seems very outdated.All in all, a good attempt. Gruesome in the right places, particularly with the deaths and creepy dream sequences but better casting and a better script might have made a better film.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Pointless remake of Richard Donner's original is technically superior in that the on-screen deaths are seamless, but it is slow going at times and young Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick overacts as the demonic Damien. The original and brilliant Jerry Goldsmith score is absent, although there is one brief Goldsmith cue played infrequently. Not a rotten movie, just a redundant one.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Heed The Review</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Five years ago senior American diplomat Robert Thorne's (Liev Schreiber) wife Katherine (Julia Stiles) had a delivery that resulted in the death of their newborn child. The thing is, she doesn't know anything about it. The hospital priest, Father Spiletto (Giovanni Lambardo Radice) convinced Robert to take and raise another child born that same night whose mother died during delivery. The child's name, Damien. (Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick) Years pass and Damien is happy as well as healthy. Everything appears perfect. That is until now, beginning at his birthday party where his nanny commits suicide. The occurrences continue with warnings from a strange priest, Father Brennan (Pete Poslethwaite) claiming Robert must defeat the son of the Devil. As the story progresses and more out of the normal events take place, Robert slowly begins to piece together the puzzle and realize the horrid truth. Damien is no ordinary child, he is none other than the Anti-Christ. It is then he learns that in order to stop the dreadful catastrophe yet to occur he must kill his own son. Or so who he believed to be his own.Sounds interesting doesn't it? Perhaps it might be if the exact story hadn't been told in theaters thirty years ago. There lies one of the problems with this extremely unnecessary re-make of 'The Omen'. It is so similar in almost every way it's disappointingly scary. Aside from a few death scenes and an added 16 second dream sequence, the only real difference here is the characters have cell phones, digital cameras, &amp; the internet.The other problem, the acting. It was so close to grisly it just barely slides by as mediocre. Julia Stiles certainly did not amaze by any means. It isn't until shortly before we last see her that she almost manages to get a hold of her character and begin to come through. Possibly her next role as Glenna in 'Edmond' being released next week in select theaters will suit her better and give her a chance to shine.I really wanted this re-make to surpass it's predetermined God-awfulness, but it didn't. Director John Moore tried to go a good job here but was unable to succeed. What he couldn't seem to grasp is when you re-make a film, you're supposed to bring and present the same feeling and likeness the initial movie contained, while still making it your own and adding your own print to it. Not rehash the same one and wrap it in a new package. He wanted to bring back a classic horror film and make it great all over again, but it didn't just happen. The fact it's practically scene for scene the same as the original is mindlessly idiotic. Heed my words, see the original 1976 'Omen', not this lousy re-make. In it you'll find far better acting and an overall more enjoyable viewing experience.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I'll start off by saying, I had not seen the original Omen before seeing this film. I can honestly say I'm not influenced by the original, from what seems like most of the reviews for this lackluster thriller are.The Omen, for me, was... kinda funny. When I go to see a horror film I want to feel my heart beating it's way out of my chest. All I got was some "jump" scares. At some points I found myself chuckling out loud, something most comedy films cannot do. Maybe this movie was just not my cup of tea, but I'm utterly disappointed that such a promising thriller storyline just never came to fruition.The one thing I must say that spoiled it the most for me was Damien, the anti-Christ himself. I just cannot be intimidated by a kid whose angry face looks more like a "Gosh darn it, I pooped myself" face. Throw Damien's hissy fits into the mix, which are easily overshadowed by some supermarket run-ins, and he's laughably evil. The ONLY thing I enjoyed about Damien were his eyes. As long as I ignored his "poop face" those eyes could be piercing.Some things were done right, although not many. The nanny was spooky. Her frazzled look, bony structure, and black eyes were perfect for the part, even if I didn't care for her actions later in the movie. The gore was perfect. I can't go into details, but I loved it. I'm not a blood and guts type of guy most of the time, but I waited anxiously for each death scene. The foreshadowing style leading up to these scenes were classical and done incredibly. The style in the movie was well done. The minimal style, especially concerning the dream sequences, really fit The Omen.I intend to see the original at some point, but for now, I'm not all that impressed. The movie could have had atmosphere if not for spotty casting and some chuckles stirred up by the script. Some aspects of the movie were perfect for me. I'm a stickler for art direction and cinematography, and this film had it. Only problem is, that alone cannot carry a film that should inspire some inkling of fear. Damien was laughable. I could go much harder on him and the film as a whole if I included spoilers. I don't see how anyone could rate a film like this higher than a 5. It fails at it's only responsibility, thrilling the audience. Looking at the other reviews, this film only managed to scar the original. I'm bumping it up to a four, from a two, only because it was a very stylish film full of eye candy. At least my eyes were entertained.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A little more BOO juice would be nice...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Being a movie buff, I have seen my fair share of movies and come to watch their remakes years later. The Omen, however, is one original that I did not get to see. Perhaps this clouds my judgment on the movie in some way but perhaps it doesn't. The reason being, the main audience that are going to see this are fans of the old movie hoping to be pleased by modern filmmakers, or kids, teenagers and young adults, who just want a good scare for a measly $6.50.I went and saw The Omen a few weeks after its release and I must admit that all the talk and hype that I had heard about the movie really let me down with the final outcome of my feelings of the movie. Don't get me wrong, I didn't HATE the movie but I did enjoy other remakes better than this one (Texas Chainsaw Massacre, The Hills Have Eyes, Amityville Horror). Now let's discuss my reasons...1.) Not enough scares. Judging from the previews, it seems that there will be "jumpy" scenes littered throughout the movie but in fact there are only a few scenes that cause you to grab the person next to you, and a few more that make you cringe away if you have a weak constitution. The scenes that do startle you have enough boo juice (as I like to call it) to get your heart rate accelerated. I just wish there were more of them.2.) Phenomenal cast. I really enjoyed the filmmakers' choice for the cast. Granted Julia Stiles, I feel, didn't do the best job she could have done but I felt that Liev Schreiber really did a great job as Robert Thorn and you could even see and hear a whisper of Gregory Peck's performance. The Bristish cast was first-rate as well. The scenes with Pete Postlethwaite's character are well performed as well as David Thewlis (my favorite character in the movie) and Michael Gambon. Newcomer Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick has also won a new place in my heart as one of the creepiest, most villainous children I have ever seen in a movie. I mean he's worst than Dakota Fanning...and she gives me nightmares sometimes...3.) Minor plot holes. It seems that during the course of the movie there are a few details mentioned but are never returned upon or elaborated on. Whether this was intentional or lazy writing, it isn't made very apparent. Granted a movie can only hold so much before the audience gets bored or doesn't care but it would still be nice if a few things were touched up on.That's all really that I have to say about this remake. Although there seems to be a black and white with no gray area for this movie, this summer scare lands firmly in the gray for me. I didn't hate it and I didn't love it either. Would I see it again? Not if I had to pay for the ticket. Is it going to become a new addition to my DVD collection when it's released? Most likely...gotta love those bonus materials!!!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>(Attempted) Style over Substance</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was looking forward to seeing this version of The Omen, as the original is a favorite of mine and I was intrigued as to what John Moore could bring to the table. So I was somewhat surprised at how annoyed I felt at the end. This film is like a Big Mac - kind of fun in parts, but leaving you hollow and hungry moments later. First off, the cast. Overall, it was well cast, but Julia Stiles was a poor choice, her acting is fine, but she's just too young for this role. And as much as I love David Thewlis' work, having him decipher the biblical passages made me relapse into happy memories of him as Johnny in 'Naked' - "Are yer familiar with the book of revelations...?" What really got my goat though, was the direction. Moore obviously saw this as his opportunity to stamp his 'auteur' mark all over this - and it comes off second rate. The symbolic layers are not very deep (though I loved Damien's funky wallpaper), and enough with the red already! This was trite when M. Night did it to death - here it is as subtle as a scarlet ferret in your pants. 'Less is more' could be the mantra for the palette. It really felt as if some scenes were lovingly designed just for their style, forget plot enhancement, and don't get me started on the cinematographic choices. It worked fine for the dog attack in the graveyard, not for the stroll through the marketplace. It's annoying because I had high hopes for this movie. So now I ask myself, why is the original so much better? In a nutshell, substance over style.</fullReview>

		<username>BroadswordCallinDannyBoy</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Pretty pointless, but manages to be enjoyable</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A remake of the 1976 horror classic with Gregory Peck.The story here is exactly the same - Robert Thorn's son is born in the early morning on June 6. The boy is quiet and keeps to himself and on his 6th birthday, strange things start to happen. First a nanny commits suicide, a weird black dog shows up, and even a seemingly crazy priest tells Robert Thorn that his son is really the son of the devil. He, of course, doesn't believe a word until the evidence seems to be irrefutable.Of all the film's that didn't need a remake, Richard Donner's "The Omen" was probably at the top of the list. It was a such a well executed horror film that the saying "if it ain't broke don't fix it" seems to have been made just for it. The results that John Moore has crafted here pretty much confirm that statement, but his version isn't wholly bad and even enjoyable.A few things that Moore has spiced up are the effects, the insertion of a few nightmare scenes, and a general glossiness from beginning to end. The updated effects are most apparent in the infamous death scene of the priest and in a beheading later in the film. The scenes are just like in the original, really, and just contain a few extra money shots, so they are entertaining, but "update" in a pretty literal sense, not re-imagined, just updated. Then there is best thing about this version - a few nightmare scenes inserted throughout and they provide the film's best jump scares.A problem is that many pulse pounding scenes from the original have been dropped or seriously down played for some inexplicable reason. A beheading scene in the original was tense, built up to, and played out with slow motion and yielded a great tension. Here Moore resorts to digital effects and the timing of the scene is: BAM and it's over. In fact, many scenes seem to play out like this: a quick dose of brutality at the cost of tension and suspense. However, then there are some scenes that were violent in the original, but Moore plays them out with suspense and tension in this version.So despite being a remake that seems to have been made only to be released on 6/6/06, the said differences make it more enjoyable than if it were a real frame-by-frame remake would have been as they provide some unexpected moments. Also Marco Beltrami's main theme is quite good.On a final note, in light of recent remakes such as "The Fog" this is a work of genius. 6/10Rated R: horror violence</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Although I'm not personally a big fan of remakes I believe the only justification for making one is when the filmmakers have something new to add to the movie or want to give the movie a so called upgrade (especially in the case of scifi or action movie where a lot more can be done thanks to modern technology).Which is why fully knowing that this is a legendary horror movie and having seen the original I automatically lowered my expectations when I went in to see this movie.As I suspected this movie violated the cardinal sin of making a remake which added nothing new to the original, it is quite clear that the studios wanted to cash in on the special date 06/06/06 that presented itself.This movie has nothing new to add to the omen , the flow of events and the concept of the movie is identical to the original.The casting is so-so, the extra young ambassador to england is hard to digest and the only character who was perfect was Mia Farrow as Mrs Baylock and David Thewlis as the photographer . 		<fullReview>Releasing on the same date that bares the number of its pedigree. "The Omen" is met with high anticipation. Director John Moore, shows his talent and this genre. However, a slightly shoddy execution, parks the whole film in neutral.Beginning with a husband ( played by Liev Schreiber in a rather mixed performance ) who has learned that his wife, who is unaware of the tragedy, has lost there baby during child birth. But threw fate, he is offered another child who is without a family. So threw the husband the couple takes the young baby boy, and threw time they grow as a happy family.These scenes of showing the family growing together over time, is a told very well. Making the climax of the story even more exciting. However, upon the boy coming of age. His mother starts to become paranoid threw moment when she is being terrorized mentally by the child. Along with that, and moments that spread strange notions of there child. There whole world is completely turns upside down when a priest tells them that there child is the Antichrist.Director John Moore, tells the story very well for the most part. The film looks very good. And save for some very particular moments, the acting and characters are completely satisfactory. The only character that leads away from the pack, is the husband played by Liev Schreiber. He nails the emotional scenes that are crucial to the story very well. However, for the most part, he delivers a rather unbelievable and stale performance.Many of the moments of pure terror that comes from the film, are done very well. And none of them stoop to the by-the-number scares that movies these days fall upon.But where the movie begins to fall apart is its execution. The film during its most important parts is very well done. However, some scenes that are equally crucial to the story are thrown to the waist side, and in the end mean almost nothing. One sequence that lends to this, is when a women sacrifices herself for the child. The point is left questionable. And the story goes on like, this moment that is crucial, never happened. There is some of that hear and there threw-out the story. And it makes it pretty disjointed in the end.There are also many very strange moments of camp. Some of the death scenes in the movie really seem very out of place in a story that is supposed to be a suspense/thriller. However, they come off as being a scene that would have came out of a rather campy horror film.There are many nit-picks here and there that are completely worth mentioning. However, the story at its core is not a bad one. The writing is done very well, and the story is told in a very well done fashion when it works. However, the problems show themselves much more then they should have making a story that is decent, but not wholly satisfying.At its core "The Omen" is a very ambitious thriller. Taking a rather interesting story, and telling it in a very decent way. However, a rather mixed execution, makes for a rather mixed experience. Making "The Omen" half-sour, and equally half-sweet.</fullReview>

		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Disappointing!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I had high hopes for this remake given the quality of the cast but this is a disappointment. I don't have a problems with remakes of classic films per se (The remake of The Thomas Crown Affair was better than the original, which was pretty good in its own right, and director John Moore's remake of The Flight of the Phoenix was as equally enjoyable as its 1965 predecessor). The good news with the remake of The Omen is the story is the same as the 1976 version. But the bad news is that the story is the same as the 1976 version. So while it remains faithful to the original, if you've seen the 1976 version, you don't need to see this one. And I would recommend the 1976 version over this remake in almost every respect.Whilst this version bears a script by David Seltzer (who wrote the previous version), the story seems much less persuasive this time round. Whilst the story is far-fetched, the 1976 version made it seem believable whilst this version seems to almost highlight the plot's flaws and lack of believability. Liev Schreiber and Julia Stiles are okay but the usually reliable Michael Gambon and Pete Postlethwaite chew the scenery. Mia Farrow, however, is a great replacement for Billie Whitelaw and both she and David Thewlis win any of the acting honours going (Thewlis' death scene is the one moment that is a big improvement over the previous version). And while the music score for the new version references a few moments from Jerry Goldsmith's Oscar-winning original score, it just simply isn't in the same league. The sadly-missed Jerry Goldsmith was always a class act even in some of his lesser scores: At the time he composed The Omen, however, he was at the top of his game and the score that resulted was one of his absolute best and one of the many reasons why the original was that so much more effective than its recent remake (The Ave Satanis theme in the original was particularly effective and added immeasurably to the mood that the original evoked that is sorely lacking from the new film). However, it is in the last scene that this new version really drops the ball. In the 1976 version, Gregory Peck's Thorn carries Damien into the church to kill him on the altar with the seven knives. Just as Thorn is about to use the first knife, Damien, who has barely spoken a word throughout the whole film, calls out to him, begging him to stop "No Daddy No". At the moment when it matters, this hitherto sullen (and silent) little boy who has been responsible for the deaths of all those who threatened to expose him, suddenly seems to be nothing more than a normal child begging his father not to hurt him. You can see Peck hesitate for a moment. Is his son really the spawn of evil that he has come to believe or has he been driven to the edge of insanity by the death of his wife? Then the police marksman tell him to put the knife down. He hesitates, then decides, raises his knife and the police marksman fires. In this new version, Damien talks all the way through the film so there is no surprise when he suddenly speaks at the end to beg for his life. As a result, the scene has been robbed of all its impact. Plus the boy playing Damien this time round (Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick) just doesn't seem as evil as Harvey Stephen's Damien from the 1976 version.Ultimately, despite the improved special effects, this was just not a remake that needed to be made. Better to remake a film that tanked the first time and put right those features of the old film that needed improvement. But who in Hollywood has the guts to do that? (apart from Steven Soderbergh and "Ocean's Eleven")</fullReview>
	</review>
