		<shortDescription>Bland remake offers some visual experimentation. (spoilers)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As a remake of the 1976 movie, "The Omen" is nearly as long and as boring, with the only creepy thing really being (as expected) the child who provides very little dialog and laughable pouting. The value of the film, itself, however, is within its visuals and narrative construction. With the kind of budget the first movie never really had, the filmmakers tried their hand with some very interesting and sometimes, innovative, scene construction that appeared to be almost carefully crafted upon foreign cinema and classic American cinema out of the 1930s and 1940s, mixed with some of the avant garde of fashion and art films of today. There is such a careful and esthetic quality that seems stupidly wasted in such a poor vehicle: an American remake of a b-grade horror film. This one, however, at least was a timely whereas the theme equates politics with Devil's wrath and destruction of Man, but couldn't this have been expressed in a horror film that was of a whole new undertaking?The idea of remaking The Omen in the first place seemed immediately ludicrous, but it is no surprise that Hollywood continues to fail to offer anything original anymore. The summer blockbusters (at least, in their budget, not always their earnings) are chock full of tired sequels and even more tired blatant remakes or simple derivatives of other films. What is more sad is, that despite the filmmakers playing with these visuals, these esthetics of scene construction and execution, they still continued to revert to the standardization of the surreal dream experiences and quick "Boo" moments which really are unnecessary, except as a last ditch effort to acquiesce to the expectations of at least the easily-scared horror fans who give a scream when it happens (we had one of those in the theater). It is just a shame that, to at least present something with such a pleasing visual quality, it had to be done through something as utterly needless as a remake of The Omen.</fullReview>

		<date>2006-06-15</date>
		<username>gothic_a666</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Very faithful reenactment</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Nowadays remakes run rampant, quietly stealing their way towards the big screen, to the point they have become a sub-genre of its own: one that spawns movies across most styles, from sugary romances to crude horror flicks. In this latter category seems to be especially partial to the remake tendency: perhaps fear is a timeless phenomenon and, as such, ever capable of reinventing itself according to the specific demands of the age. Whether remakes are a redundant exercise or important re-interpretations of great classics is yet to be decided and may be a question that cannot be settled unilaterally but be a matter of individual worth. The Omen (2006) is as faithful as a remake could ever hope to be. It is true that it is not a frame-by-frame carbon copy, but it retains the same narrative structure, virtually unscathed and delivers an imagetic scheme that is highly reminiscent of the original. Anyone familiar with the first Omen will be immediately reminded of some of the most impacting scenes, that have been translated to this new effort with little or not alternation in terms of form. What does change is the approach. This production is glossy and clearly stylish, the setting is explored through a multi-layered offer of different angles. Closeups introduce occasional detailed renderings of the characters, a bit like short lived character studies. In a pleasant surprise, the Omen does almost away with the nagging habit of extreme editing that seems to cripple so many contemporary fiction. ***Spoiler***It is particularly praise-worthy the scene in which a nurse, surrounded in light, holds the baby Damien, while his father-to-be looks at him from the shadows behind a glass wall. It was almost choreographed to capture and retain the viewer's attention. ***Spoiler***In terms of acting, it was competent and sober. Characters' profiles deviate slightly from the original, enough to allow us to glimpse the actor behind. This is notorious in the case of Damien himself: in this movie he is much more distant than in the former effort, to the point he seems to border on autism.The movie does manage to create a certain brooding atmosphere that lurks just beyond what is seen, this owing some to the dream sequences that far from original are still interesting enough. But overall, it is the contrast of dark, moody settings interspersed with cold and aesthetic shots that creates the tension necessary to unroll the scrolls of a horror story. Like the first, The Omen (2006) is more interested in suspense that in supernatural manifestations. Yet the death scenes that were the hallmark of the great original have not omitted, in fact they remain very similar: only updated to fit the 21st century. Which might have done more harm than good, these shocking events are too much like what one expect from the semi-serious Final Destination series to reap any admiration.But overall, there is a feeling of lingering disquiet, of general awkwardness, something too vague to be properly defined. A movie like this cannot help but be compared, its existence seems to demand such a comparison, to be almost part of its deeper meaning. The 2006 production does not top the original, in fact, one wonders if it ever tried to. While the credibility of remakes is at stake and still an issue of much debate, this particular movie seems to be a work of emulation. Clearly, the director admired and studied the Omen universe, adapting it to his personal view, while quoting many of the elements that make this one of the greatest horror movies ever done. It's more a work of skillful invocation than original creation. For those familiar with the first movie, this newer version might create a feeling of watered down intentions, an all too clear token of all that cannot be recreated. Yet it may also remind the same viewers of how much actually can be recaptured and delivered, according to a spirit of admiration nothing short of reverence.Perhaps not an icon for times to be, The Omen (2006) is a reminder that horror can still exist on its own, without falling prey to the ever growing trends of shallow minded productions that, unfortunately, abound.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>An average, badly miscast remake.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I think that if I were a director I would hate to remake a movie, especially one that is as well known as The Omen. I wanted to give this movie a chance, it seemed that this time, there was some actual talent involved in a remake. I have to say that the original is a movie that has always scared me. I first saw it as a kid and I saw it on DVD just a couple of days ago, and it always manages to creep me out. The original was done really well, it was more creepy than scary, but it stays with you after you watch it. And what can I say about the music, no other film score that I have heard can compare to this one for sheer terror. I can't listen to Jerry Goldsmith's score, especially the piece "Ave Satani" without getting the chills! It made the film.Now to the new one. It suffers from what any remake suffers from, a constant comparison to the original by the viewer. The movie uses the original screenplay by David Seltzer, with only a few tweaks, it is almost a shot by shot remake. The script was so good, that I can see why they wanted to use it again. The biggest problem here is the casting. It is all wrong. When I saw the trailer months ago I suspected that Liev Schreiber and Julia Styles were miscast, and I feel I was right. They are competent actors, but THEY ARE TOO YOUNG!!!! Especially Schreiber, he is too young and dull to come close to the elegance and pathos that Gregory Peck brought to the role of Robert Thorne. Styles just seems to be going through the motions here. The nanny Mrs. Baylock is played by Mia Farrow, she is fine, but is not nearly as creepy as the original lady that played her Billie Whitelaw. I could go on, the only role that stands out is Father Brennan played by Pete Postlethwaite.But the absolute biggest casting mistake is Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick as Damien. In the original, the kid played Damien was very creepy, even though he was shown really as a normal kid, unaware of who he was. His look was creepy, his stare was creepy. The new Damien, is not scary or creepy, he appears more in the film and as one of the tweaks in the script has more lines. He seems to be aware of who he is, and that takes away from the films effectiveness. He really comes off as annoying. Saying that there are some things in the film that work to make it at least an average viewing. This film has some pretty effective "jump out at you scares", and the action sequences, especially at the end of the film are done well. This film will definitely be better to someone who hasn't seen the 1976 version. The story is still good, so it is never boring. As a whole, I can only say that it is an average film. It proves that the right casting and a good director is as important as a script, Richard Donner made a great horror classic with the same script, John Moore is no Richard Donner, and it shows.I can definitely say that I enjoyed it more than Da Vinci Code.Grade: C</fullReview>
	</review>

The Omen is no exception to this rule, with its dreadful script, disappointing cast, and uninspired direction. and the way it sticks so close the original Omen film that it is a near shot-by-shot remake.Liev Schreiber practically sleep-walks through the whole movie and has some sort of perpetually confused expression on his face. So, they used a classic film, used no class in adapting it to fit with current catastrophes, hired a dull yes-man director, and decided that it would be cool to release a movie on 6-6-06. 		<shortDescription>Would be fantastic except it's a remake</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If there was ever an example of cinema as product then this is it. I doubt this film would have ever been made except to cash in on the date 06/06/06. When I first heard about this effort, I had some slight hope that a whole new story would be constructed around the basic premise of the original (like what happened with the remake of 'Planet of the Apes' a few years back). Not a chance. Instead, this is basically a reshoot of the 1976 horror epic that starred Gregory Peck and Lee Remick as an affluent couple who unknowingly adopt the son of the Devil. This time around it's Liev Schreiber and Julia Stiles who are the unfortunate parents of demonic brat Damien.There are some new elements added to the plot: the boy Damien seems to be playing an active part in the mayhem that is unleashed on his behalf (a fact that adds a chill to the final scene), there is a prologue in the Vatican observatory indicating that the Catholic hierarchy are aware of the coming of the AntiChrist (a genuinely thought-provoking and eerie scene to be fair), and some wicked dream sequences get thrown into the mix for good measure.Otherwise, screenwriter David Seltzer pretty much regurgitates the script he wrote for the original film (I wonder what he got paid for this, talk about money for nothing!). This destroys much of the suspense and foreboding that a story like this really needs. SPOILER: Anyone who's seen the 1976 version will find themselves experiencing a severe case of deja vu- the entire plot unfolds in exactly the same way and big chunks of dialogue appear to have been unaltered also.However there are some positive points, the soundtrack is very effective and underlines the gloomy, doom-laden atmosphere that prevails. There are about five or six really top-notch shock moments scattered throughout the movie. In addition, the film is visually stunning with striking photography, outstanding production design, and gorgeous and effectively used locations. I loved the film's use of vivid color to set the emotional tone. I was engrossed by this movie even though I was irritated at how predictable it was.The cast really make their best effort with the material they've been given. Pete Postelthwaite and David Thewlis are scene stealers, and Mia Farrow is wonderful as Damien's Nanny- imagine a Satanic Mary Poppins and you're kind of on the right track. Both Liev Schreiber and Julia Stiles offer effective portrayals of Damien's ever-more beleaguered parents, and Schrieber does a good job of making his character's motivations more ambiguous than Gregory Peck's were in the original. The viewer is left wondering whether he takes action against Damien to save humanity from the AntiChrist, or merely to save his own skin. The death scenes are luridly well-executed, and the Rottweillers from Hell are amazing.If I had never seen the original, I would probably say that this was one of the best horror movies that I have seen in years. Instead it's like hearing a cover version of a favorite song- there's no freshness but it's reassuringly familiar.</fullReview>
	</review>

If you have seen the Original and Liked it then you will most likely enjoy your time spent watching this film as it stays very true to the original and in some cases the cinematography and mise-en-scene are identical. The film is a basic carbon copy of the original but obviously updating a classic to appeal to a new generation of movie goers, it even includes references to certain modern day disasters such as 9/11 and the 2004 Tsunami to show the coming of Armageddom brought upon us as part of the cycle ending in Damiens birth onto this earth.I felt the biggest floor in the film was the casting.I felt the child cast as Damien, a very simple role reliant on looks more than anything, didn't look menacing enough and at times i felt he'd be better in a Home Alone style of film. Mia Farrow was excellent as the Nanny as i felt she had quite an artificial, sickly and sometimes sadistic manor and suited the role perfectly. But thats my opinion. Stiles and Schraeber do competent jobs as the parents but I don't feel either stole the show or acted superbly.Visually i loved this film, and a few sequences in particular were very very well done. As for the scares, there aren't many at all, and a few could be seen coming a mile away (dream sequence/mirror open then shuts to see apparition in rear,etc..) I did jump physically in one sequence where i wasn't expecting it (which i won't mention specifically so it may surprise you too). There is a distinct feel of Final Destination to certain moments in this movie as you will see, but the average gore fan out there shouldn't be disappointed with the moments of blood here.So, it was an entertaining 2 hours. Nothing terribly new, and not as creepy or Gothic as the first one. In a way, i was hoping it might go in a different direction, but perhaps, it's the producers' ultimate compliment to the original - keep it very similar,just updating it for a new generation of horror fans. Recommended but don't expect fireworks.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>better that I expected, but still a pointless remake (plot details mentioned)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This Hollywood obsession with remaking horror movies from the past continues with The Omen. Coming to cinemas after the releases this year of The Fog (awful) and When A Stranger Calls (even worse), I wasn't expecting much. Also, Hollywood has a very poor record recently of making good and scary horror movies. So with low expectations I went to see this........and in truth it's not bad. But it's not good either. The plus signs, are that the plot is the same with no new twists or spins on it that I can remember. The death scenes are very well done, especially the decapitation scene. While watching it, I kept thinking about the deaths in the Final Destination series, as they were done in similar ways (although I did wonder if the makers of the Final Destination movies, stole the 'accidental' death idea from the deaths in the original Omen trilogy). The actors are pretty good too.However...The original did not need to be remade. I also feel that if you are remaking, re-imagining, or updating, or whatever you call it, you need to do something to engage modern viewers. The Dawn Of The Dead update showed you can take the same plot and change it enough to be different, while retaining the key elements of the original. For The Omen remake, as I understand it, they simply took the original screenplay and pretty much stuck with it, without changes. If you are going to do that, then what's the point of remaking it? I want to see that script, I'll watch the original thank you.The actors, as I said are good, but the cast simply can't compete with the original actors. Gregory Peck v Liev Schrieber? Lee Remick v Julia Stiles? David Warner v David Thewlis? No contest. And then there was the child playing Damien... The scowl on his face all movie just made him look like a spool brat, not the anti-Christ! The child in the original was much better.A couple of bizarre, and frankly pointless dream sequences didn't really add anything either, so I'm not sure why they were there.The original Omen is a good horror movie (not a classic in my view). This remake will probably make more money, possibly mean the sequel Damien: Omen II will be remade soon, but it won't linger in the minds of horror fans like the original does.Despite all this however, I feel as a Friday night movie, it works. Just don't expect to spend Saturday and onwards thinking about it.</fullReview>

		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Pointless</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was hoping for a good update of a decent classic horror film. Instead I got a pointless remake ala Gus Vanzant's "Psycho" (1998). I longed for the fast forward button so that I could skip the expository dialogue scenes in which I already knew what was to be said because I had seen the 1976 version. The only interesting thing was to see how famous scenes would be updated. The zoo scene with the monkeys was actually less effective than in the original. The original's claustrophobic effect of being in a car surrounded by raging animals is lost perhaps for the better because it would now seem like a retread of "Jurassic Park" (1993). There are a few more inserted shots of frighteningly made up people, similar to what was introduced by "The Exorcist" (1973) and perfected by Japanese horror films, but these inserts do not add a whole lot. The death "accidents" have been updated with more post processing flash and explosions, but it seems to take away from their power. Overall nothing is added (the internet is now used a little) and I see no real reason for remaking a 30 year old film in this way. If you have seen the original, you may as well re-watch it or wait for this film to come to DVD. If you have never seen the original, you may still find this enjoyable, but you may be better off renting the DVD of the 1976 version.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>The original Omen has a very fond spot in my memory from childhood. I remember seeing the poster at the video store and the simple chilling power it had over me as I was asked by my folks to hurry up and select a film. I think it was the crow on the poster that did it. Followed by the tormented faces of Gregory Peck and Lee Remick leering back at me. It reminded me so much of Alfred Hitchcock's The Birds (we'll to an eight year old it did) and that thought terrified me. It also excited me.I was not allowed to choose The Omen according to the video clerk (due to it in its day being quite controversial and the South African censors so narrow minded that they slapped it with an age restriction of no persons under 21). How was a eight year old going to get to see this obviously very scary movie that had adults only written all over it? Well thank goodness I never had narrow minded parents (that only happened later on when Basic Instinct finally arrived, also set at 21 age restriction, and not being able to watch it because it had orgasms in it( I was 15 then)). Parents are funny things! Well anyway (back to childhood), a few weeks later Dad had brought it home and I was allowed to watch if I promised not to take it all seriously (I know this is asking a lot from such a young child most psychologists would say) but I loved them more for it. They trusted me that I was old enough and big enough to handle what was only a film and we discussed it religiously afterwards to debunk any fears I may have formed regarding the film and our faith (we are devoted Christians). It struck chords of delightful terror in me and when I think back I smile with pure cinematic joy at the three of us huddled together being scared out of our wits.So of course I was one of the first to rush out to see the remake. I wanted to relive that moment so many years before, so I tried to drag Mom and Dad with me to see it. They had since moved on (horror no longer did it for them but Mrs. Henderson Presents...yes dear any day) so I took a friend who'd never seen the original. The premise of the rise of the anti-Christ from the vocal point of his childhood did nothing for my friend who pushed it aside as pure Hollywood sensationalism (due to it being released on 06.06.2006). For me, it was like being transported back fifteen or so years. As I watched the film certain scenes from the remake sparked of synapses in memory of the original an all I could do was smile. But time has moved on and at age 28 the original storyline no longer has the power or grip it once held over me in childhood. I actually found myself chuckling at some of the absurdities that had clearly passed me bye originally. That is not to say I did not enjoy it. As far as remakes go this was a commendable job and as far as memory serves me, very faithful to its source (the one death scene involving the beheading of one of the characters has changed dramatically). Perhaps horror no longer has its sway on my imagination like it once did but if for only a few hours, this new remake did allow me to relive a part of childhood I cherish.6/10 Rated no persons under 16 for Violence in South Africa</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Actually, this is one of the better remakes. It's quite entertaining and creepy, has a great cast and one of the most eye popping beheadings I have ever seen. Beside that, where's the point in remaking a classic like Richard Donner's 1976 version. Apart from the visual effects, there is nothing the remake can improve on. Instead of this, you realize what an important part of the first one Jerry Goldsmith's music plays in setting up mood and suspense. Marco Beltrami's score may be good for those who don't know the original, but it is nowhere near the impact of the first. The cast ranges from effective to good and Mia Farrow is great in the Billie Whitelaw role of Mrs. Baylock. Production values are good and the camera-work is fine (Only one of those dumb jittery hand-held sequences which I hate from the bottom of my heart!) and overall it's a great looking film. All in all, there's nothing to be ashamed of but there is also nothing to congratulate to. Maybe Hollywood should better concentrate on remaking bad or mediocre originals like Amityville Horror, The hills have eyes or Texas Chainsaw Massacre instead of films that still deliver the goods, even after 30 years. For me, The Omen has well and truly been forgiven, forgiven for its sin of being reborn in the hands of Director John Moore, of Behind Enemy Lines (2001) and Flight of the Phoenix (2004) fame. He has nurtured his newly adopted child and given us a holy new movie that can stand on his own two feet, with a very reassuring British cast driving this movie forward with dexterity and personality, such as David Thewlis, Pete Postlethwaite and Michael Gambon. But as with the troughs, we have a very one-dimensional lead role and his non-existent wife, these being the weakest link and with some of the dullest character development since Bill Pullman and his Presidential days in Independence Day (1996), truly dire and lifeless. With this calibre of English actors, why have the American market let this movie down? Is there not a single actor and actress of this calibre that, ironically, Hollywood can claim to have brought forward? No doubt Liev Schreiber and Julia Stiles were not the first choice?The cinematography, done by Jonathan Sela, has delivered us from a seemingly slow start, to a pace that has all the trademarks of a very dark and frightening world that is the fight between good and evil, with its forbidden pastures, and the consequence of trespass that this revelation brings. The Omen will have us believe that the Worlds past, and future, disasters are not merely man made, but driven by a Higher force, such an easy excuse to even blame both Good and Evil. With this predicament in mind, The Omen delivers itself, kicking and screaming, into a New World of impending doom and chaos, with the pace slowly building up to a fine mixture of paranoia, death and bleak fatefulness.Yes, this was a great little movie, a modern day horror with Historical overtones. I really did enjoy it, with its moments of fright, shock and mild horror, with a little bit of Soul searching; this latest edition to the Omen family should thrill and entertain.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>I might be biased, but this remake is so much better than the original that it simply saved the Omen experience for me.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As a person born in 1985, I find it very difficult to enjoy most films from the 70's and 80's (besides Disney's animated features). Yet, as a devout Horror fan, I realize that many of the classics are from that time, and have taken it upon myself to familiarize myself with at least some of them. I'd like to open this review with a special thanks to director John Moore, who has salvaged The Omen experience for me. It might be my fault for not liking 70's acting and cinematography, but I simply didn't like the way the original 1976 film was made. This 2006 remake has taken the fine (if slightly too religious, and affiliated with Christianity in specific rather than religion in general) story and script and has fixed anything and everything that needed fixing!The acting was so much better than in the original. Having great actors like Liev Schreiber and Julia Stiles (both whom I happen to like from their prior films) was just a bonus I guess, as acting better than the original cast would be easy for you and me as well. The face expressions, the "struggle" scenes, the dialogues, everything was so much more realistic and believable. Even more importantly - little Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick was a far better Damien. With barely having 3 lines in the entire film, his penetrating blue eyes, his mature and almost old expression and his body language were all just perfect for the part. He wasn't scary or creepy, he was dark and gloomy.The screenplay and script were hardly touched, though some of the scenes were altered a little (and each time for the better!), and some of the shots became a lot clearer making it easier to understand the events. So anyone who isn't much of 70's cinema fan could watch this one instead of the original, though I'd still recommend watching all Omen films by chronological order.All in all - I can't emphasize enough how much this production shined compared to the original. The Omen of 2006 is a fine film, but compared to The Omen of 1976 it's simply fantastic. Very happy I decided to watch it despite not enjoying the original! Rated 6 as a film and 9 in comparison with the original, final average rating 7. The Omen has been redeemed!</fullReview>

Perhaps the greatest ploy of all time, The Omen remake could not have been released at a better time. Not only did it have the 6/6/06 release date to its advantage, but in a time when 70s classics are being remade into gorefests (Hills Have Eyes, Texas Chainsaw Massacre, Black Christmas), it was time for a remake that relied on more than shock value. Under normal circumstances, I'd whine about how The Omen being remade is pointless, how it's all about money, etc. though ultimately when the film it theaters, I was tired of every R-rated horror flick being about blood and sadism than genuine scares. To my surprise, I enjoyed this remake, even more so the second time around. It's almost scene for scene the same picture as the original, only not as good. Although director John Moore is no Richard Donner, he still impresses with plenty of slick shots, effective shaky cam moments, and when he wants, a style other than Donner's. If only he went with his own style more, this remake could have been a classic in its own right. As it stands, it's a well done horror remake, and one of the best horror pictures of 2006. 7/10</fullReview>

I can't really say anything in this movie is better than the original, because it's the same. The camera focuses on inanimate objects that seem to take on a life of their own. There is danger everywhere around us is what the message seems to be.Get ready to enjoy the dark ride.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2006-06-07</date>
		<username>Dustin Johnson (hawrnball)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>As far as remakes go, it's not that bad.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I had two reasons for seeing The Omen: 1) I love horror movies and 2) morbid curiosity. As a rule, I am staunchly against the idea of remakes but every so often, like every rule, there are exceptions. This is one of them.The acting ranges from barely mediocre to very decent. I'm not sure why but as a general I have a fondness for Liev Shreiber. He's honest, charming, confident, likable and he doesn't disappoint here. He portrays Robert Thorn with such quiet intensity that if anyone could replace the immortal Gregory Peck, it's him.Let me be blunt: Julia Stiles is miscast as Katherine Thorn and I knew it from the second I first saw her in the trailer. Her character is supposed to be in her mid-forties and not so much a young trophy wife. The part is altered to fit Stiles' age but somehow, it still doesn't work. Her and Robert are now about ten years apart and there's no real chemistry between them, no spark. All that aside, Stiles can't be blamed for these issues. She plays the hand she's been dealt and does well with what she's been given. She goes from cheerful and proud new mother, to suspicious, to downright bitchy, to frightened and pulls it all off believably. Furthermore, it was rather refreshing to see her tackle a role that's remarkably different than what she's played in the past.Mia Farrow is a fabulous Mrs. Baylock, hitting the opposite end of the spectrum of the role (the "desperate housewife" Rosemary) that launched her into fame. Her role is all about irony what with her kind, assuring face, silky, soothing voice and a sort of elderly innocence that all hide something much more sinister.David Thewlis is pitch perfect as the ambitious photographer. His performance is not particularly award worthy but everything about it is spot on and believable.Unfortunately, Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick is not very good as Damien and the film suffers because of it. This not so promising child actor tries much too hard to appear menacing and demonic. Not once does he smile in the film (except in the last shot) and all he does is scowl and glower. It was much more frightening in the original when Harvey Stephens portrayed Damien as a fairly normal child: cheerful, playful, carefree, innocent. Here, we are bludgeoned with the fact that this child is evil and I grew weary of it. After a while, I didn't even buy Davey-Fitzpatrick as the anti-Christ but just as a snotty little brat that was in desperate need of a slap.John Moore's direction is slick and competent; he is able to effectively establish the mood and general feeling of tension and dread. At times, however, he resorts to hitting the audience with cheap shot jump scenes accompanied by jolts in music and amplified sound effects--predictable ones I might add. There were too many moments when I was thinking, "Uh oh, it's really quiet right now, something's going to pop out. Yup, there it is." I rolled my eyes, but the kids in my audience bought into it. What I find scary is when I am expecting something to jump out when the scene dead silent and there's a lot of build but it never amounts to anything. Moore never does that here.Technically speaking, everything seems to be top-notch: editing, sound, sets, costumes, and especially the camera-work which I found was done particularly well. It's frenzied and claustrophobic when it needs to be as well as slow-moving and deliberate when it needs to be. The musical score is not as nerve-jangling as Jerry Goldsmith's overbearing compositions (and "Ave Satani" is notably absent throughout the film but makes an audible appearance in the closing credits) but is still effective, creepy and sometimes understated enough to compliment what we see on the screen.Screenwriter David Seltzer (who also wrote the original 1976 screenplay) updates the story into modern times and certain world events of the past few years have been incorporated into the plot as prophecies told in the Book of Revelations appear to be coming true; it is rather unsettling, no matter what your religious views. This is a somewhat different take on a story that draws influence from its source. Characters, scenes, situations, and methods of demise (Lee Remick plummeting to her death was gruesome but watching a bubble of air slowly work its way through Julia Stiles' IV and into her bloodstream was enough to make me squirm) are altered so that the result is something other than a simply updated version of an older story (like Gus Van Sant's puzzling shot-for-shot remake of Psycho). This new version is able to exist as a fairly independent entity but still remains faithful and Seltzer treats his own material with respect.The film stuck with me for a bit afterward and I realized how much I actually liked it the more I brooded on it. I paid full price to see it (ten bucks) and I did feel like I got my money's worth. I even thought it was worth viewing a second time and that right there is a good sign. It is definitely not a half-assed remake; it looks as though a lot of serious work went into this picture in front of and behind the camera and the result is something worth a recommendation.While the film does get knocked down a few pegs just for being a remake, it also suffers from some preventable flaws. It is inevitably inferior to its thirty-year-old predecessor but it does not disappoint as much as I expected it to.I give The Omen a 6 out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I have to say that I was pleasantly surprised by this flick. I went to see it last night (6/6/06... The death scenes are pretty horrific and are basically the same deal as the original, maybe just a bit more gruesome this time around. David Thewlis's decapitation scene was really well done and is at least on par with David Warner's horrific death form the original. Speaking of Thewlis, I thought he was fun to watch. He almost has that David Warner charm. Mia Farrow was great as the nanny from hell. They made some interesting changes to the storyline like adding scenes of destruction from recent headlines, most notably the attack on the World Trade Center. The movie is almost exactly the same as the original, just a little more spiffed up. Oh and before I forget, I jumped twice while watching this. The original never actually did that for me.Problems I had with the film start with the casting. Julia Stiles was, in my opinion, way too young looking for the role. They needed someone with a little more age to play the part. That's not to say she was bad, she was just not convincing in the part. Liev Schreiber didn't bring me into the film the way Gregory Peck did and there were only few moments where he managed to shine. The kid who played Damien... he was good but I think one of the problems with the film is that from the start, this kid is an evil little bastard. Yeah, he's the Anti-Christ and all that jazz but part of what made the original so creepy was that the kid really had no idea who he was and was seemingly innocent and ambiguous. The Damien of the remake gives dirty looks to everyone, shows up in people's nightmares and participates even more in his mother's demise this time around. I don't know why but I just preferred it when the kid wasn't outright evil like this kid was. The music in this film is sadly, nonexistent. Considering that Jerry Goldsmith won an Oscar for the original's score, they should have given the score a little more heft. Last but not least, the ending, while just about the same as the original, was not done right. All I can say is that they edited the scene wrong. It's not like the original, where you don't know exactly what happened to Thorn and his son until the very last shot. A little weak, in my opinion.This was a decent remake and I liked the fact that they had respect for the original because it definitely shows. They stuck with what worked and tweaked some things here and there, even if they goofed with some considerably important elements. Worth a watch but it's nothing you have to run to the theater to see.RATING: *** out of *****.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>If you haven't seen the original Omen film it is well worth checking out as a benchmark of horror excellence. Unfortunately, as with most modern remakes, they fail to live up to expectation or quality control and The Omen, although better than most, is still an unwarranted and unnecessary updated version of a much loved classic.In place of an atmospheric and chilling set-up, the remake relies on cheap horror tricks such as creepy dream sequences ending with a loud noise to make the audience jump which I find obtrusive and ill-fitting. The answer is of course money. So save yourself the price of a cinema ticket and rent the original if you haven't seen it or go and see something else.</fullReview>

		<date>2006-08-30</date>
		<username>Phantasm01</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Good remake</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>First of all, I'm not much of a fan of the original, which is slow and turgid and often teeters on the edge of boredom. There's not enough wrong with it for me to dislike it, but there's not enough good about it to make me like it either. It stands in no-man's land. The remake is much more fun, though it suffers from some of the same concerns, such as uneven pacing and a basic plot that just isn't that hot. How, you might rightly ask, does the remake manage to be much more entertaining than the original despite following it incredibly closely? Well... it's tough to say. The budget plays a big part in it, honestly. Watching people sit around, or wander around to different locales is a lot more appealing when you can make it look really good in the process. It's also helped, somewhat, by the performances. Mia Farrow is fun and over the top as the creepy nanny. The colder performance in the original was fine in and of itself, but this goofier turn adds a bit of life to the film. Furthermore, the central character of Robert Thorn is much better this time around. This isn't to say Schreiber gives a great performance; he's merely okay, a little dull, frankly. But his turn worked much better than Peck's original did, as Peck made Thorn into a pompous buffoon whom I actively disliked. (Peck also managed to overact without actually conveying any emotion. That's quite a feat.) I don't feel an overwhelming amount of sympathy for this Thorn, but not hating him does help the film, as he is the protagonist and all. (And, as the film goes along and things get worse and worse I did start to sympathize with him a bit.) Some Seamus kid plays Damien, and he's been much criticized, and not without some reason. One of the relative strengths of the original was that it didn't try and make Damien be all scary and evil, what with that being a pretty tall order for a little kid. Here Seamus mostly just glowers, umm, evilly, and he is something less than terrifying. It coulda been worse, though, and he doesn't get too much screen time. Julia Stiles takes over the role of Kate Thorn, and she has an extraordinarily round and squishy looking face. I didn't notice too much else about her. (I must say, however, that her performance was not conspicuously bad, as I anticipated it might be.) Also of note is Pete Postelthwaite as one of the various crazy priests, Father Brennan. It's the standard Postelthwaite role, twitchy and creepy and overdone, but I've always liked it before, and he continues to entertain me now. Lastly, though he doesn't do much, I couldn't help but mention the appearance of Italian horror veteran Giovanni Lombardo Radice as another priest. I hate to be the guy who gets interested when some smalltime actor from obscure, forgotten film randomly shows up in something big unexpectedly, but his appearance did amuse me. This time around Radice gets his face melted, though we don't actually see it happen on screen...Again, this is a big-budget affair, particularly for a horror film, coming in with a budget 30 times greater than that of the original. It's all up their on the screen, and the film looks great. Sorry folks, but the money does help a lot. Take, for example, the graveyard scene. The scene is pretty much the same in both versions in terms of content, but the new one is still multitudes better because it actually looks good, as they had the time and money to find an appropriately ominous setting. This could be said of most of the film, as a matter of fact. It's an odd combination really, the somewhat silly plot and often wacky performances combine with an ultra-sharp look, making this film classy and hokey at the same time. Also reducing the hokiness factor somewhat is the absence of the lame Gothic sounding music of the original. What they replaced it with is undistinguished, but at least it doesn't hurt the film.The various death scenes, like most of the rest of the film, are largely the same, aside from the sharper visual style. This isn't uniformly true however, as their is a bit of not so great CGI work, but there ain't much of it overall. Most notably, the decapitation scene has been reworked to some degree. Nowadays a decap isn't liable to impress anyone too much, but it is pretty well done, anyway. The impalation was the best death the first time around, I thought, and it's pretty cool here too. Probably the best one. It does have a bit of the aforementioned unfortunate CGI, but it makes up for it in other ways.All that said, I'm not really sure who the audience for this film is. Horror aficionados will, for the most part, dislike it for the modern aspects, and hate it for being a remake. And I dunno if it'll appeal to stupid teenagers too much either, probably finding it too slow (as I did, frankly) and moody. (It does make some concessions to the newer style of horror, but I don't think enough to win most of them over.) Nevertheless, I thought it was fun, if far from remarkable. So I guess you gotta be like me, having a taste for both modern and 70's style horror (the golden age of horror films, if ever there was one), and a sufficient affection for the genre to take the good aspects of the film while not being especially bothered by the bad parts. If you can do that, you'll probably have a good time.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>SPOILERS!!! Liev Schreiber may never be Gregory Peck, but he is a terrific actor in his own right and here plays a U.S. ambassador who inadvertently sets Armageddon in motion by allowing an orphaned infant to be substituted for his stillborn son. The substitute kid is actually part of a scheme to bring The Devil Himself back to Earth. Truth is, I thought Gregory Peck was too old for the role when the original was made back in the early 1970s, although he certainly gave a dignified if somewhat wooden performance. Schreiber seems more age-appropriate and animated to me. In the Lee Remick role, Julia Stiles holds her own as the ambassador's increasingly disturbed wife, and it is a pleasure to see her playing a mature adult. Considering the various abrupt and sometimes gruesome deaths, Stiles' death scene is sorrowful and horrifying, and almost impossible to watch. The kid playing Damien is almost identical to the original, and he is quite convincing as a devilish tyke. The race-to-the-finish ending is a little hard to swallow. Other than that, this remake of THE OMEN turns out to be a major and pleasant surprise considering all the other recent horror film remakes that have been dogs (THE FOG, anyone?). I predict strong box office for this.</fullReview>

		<date>2006-06-06</date>
		<username>rparham</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>Avoid this Omen</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Omen is the reason remakes get such a bad reputation, because it is one of the worst movies of recent memory to make it to a major multiplex. Not mediocre, not middling, but downright, out and out bad. The film has almost nothing to recommend it and everything to reward you for avoiding it at all costs.The Omen begins in Rome, where Vatican astronomers have seen ominous comets in the sky, and along with various other events (such as the World Trade Center bombing and massive hurricanes), have determined that Armageddon is just around the corner. It is at this time, on June 6 to be precise, that Robert Thorn (Liev Schreiber), deputy U.S. Ambassador to Italy, discovers that his wife, Katherine (Julia Stiles) has had a stillborn child. A priest in the hospital offers Robert a shred of hope: another child has been born to a mother who died during childbirth, and the babies are switched without anyone knowing. So, the Thorns begin raising Damien (Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick) and then tragedy strikes: the Ambassador to Italy is promoted to Ambassador to Great Britain, but is then killed in a freak accident and Robert takes over as his duties.Shortly after the move to London, more strange things begin happening: Damien's nanny hangs herself at Damien's birthday party and a crazed priest (Pete Postlethwaite) tells Robert that his son is the son of the Devil and that he will kill the entire family to ensure his ascension to power. The priest shortly meets with a rather grisly fate. Robert doesn't believe the priest at first, but a photographer (David Thewlis) who has taken pictures of both the nanny and the priest notices strange artifacts in the photos: shadows of images that suggest violence occurring to the individuals in the same way they died. Into the fold arrives Damien's new nanny, Mrs. Baylock (Mia Farrow), who is almost obsessive in her protection of Damien, going so far as to bring an attack dog into the house. When more strange events begin happening around Damien, Katherine is certain there is something wrong with her child, and Robert decides to take action.On the face of it, The Omen would seem to be competently made: the cinematography is attractive, even stylish at moments and it's obvious some money was spent on the production. However, this is offset by almost every other element of the film; The Omen unfolds in a long, dreary slog to anti-climax.For those who would argue that remakes on their very face are unnecessary, The Omen gives plenty of fuel to the fire of that point of view. There are no major differences between this version and the original, it is an almost exact replica of the 1976 film, and therefore if you have seen that entry already, there is nothing unique here, and you have seen it all done immensely better.Director John Moore has made The Omen serious to the point of ridiculous; everything is served up without a moment of levity. The film's stolid tone is supposed to lead you to believe that we are watching "important" events unfold when in fact we are viewing some rather cheesy stuff. Add to that the fact that Marco Beltrami's score is dramatic in the extreme, pushing every key moment over the top. There is no subtlety to The Omen, no sense of creepy foreboding. Moore does craft some slightly effective dream sequences and a few decent shock scares, but they are few and far between, and every other moment generates not an iota of suspense. What you are left with is a film that never seems to end, but merely moves forward by sheer momentum alone and you find yourself wondering when it is all going to be over.The leads, Schrieber and Stiles, are stiff and wooden; their emotions and reactions are mostly one note, and their dialogue delivery is generally poor. There is no empathy for these people, they are so without energy or basic acting skill, it would seem, so as to lead you to think you are watching amateurs in a high school play. It is impossible to believe these two as real, living beings and not actors reading from a script. The capper is reserved for Davey-Fitzpatrick as Damien. He is easily the worst child actor in a major film production in some time, because this role requires from him some serious facial emoting, but the best he can come up with is a rather ridiculous, pouty face that is not the least bit menacing, but in fact overly humorous. The only good elements in the film are Mia Farrow and Pete Postlethwaite. They are both effective in their respective roles, although they have limited screen time to make much of an impression, but at least they are diverting to watch. Thewlis is adequate but unspectacular and Michael Gambon manages to use his two minutes of screen time to chew scenery with abandon, since there are no "small" moments to The Omen.An important footnote is that in the theater where this film unspooled, the projector was obviously misadjusted to allow the viewing of boom microphones and set lights, and even a scene with a camera matte box top visible in the frame. The audience had a grand old time with this, laughing at the film throughout. Unfortunately, if the film had been properly projected, it wouldn't have made a difference, because it is such as loss as to not matter. The Omen is one long exercise in tedium, and those who sit through this film will find themselves wondering if the filmmakers are the sons of the Devil for putting the audience through it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Droll, hollow and mostly uninteresting; there are some jumps and some smirks, as we recall the original, but so what?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Omen is probably a bit better than the average horror film update; remake and reboots that we've had to endure in recent times, but this is as far as praise can realistically go when speaking of it. Penned by the very man who was responsible for the previous three (as well as a 1990's direct to TV special) in David Seltzer, although directed by someone with a rooted interest in updating; remaking and adapting in John Moore, this 2006 revisiting to a pretty spooky 1976 Richard Donner thriller ticks boxes, goes through motions and carries with it a shinier surface - a mixture of exactly what we remember form before (via Seltzer) but with that distinct, polished "bringing it all into the 21st Century" feel of one of Moore's pieces. The question is, as it always is, is: was there really ever any need? No, of course there wasn't; but a marketers dream is to go through all the PR paraphernalia over a film about the coming of The Devil that's due to be released on the sixth day of the sixth month, two-thousand and six. It's superfluous; a tad dull and sexes up some of the more underplayed moments of the '76 original, but is workmanlike.First and foremost, the opening credits are straight out of David Fincher's 1995 masterpiece "Se7en". Painfully so, in fact and that sense of things being rebranded; updated - that whole sense of seeing things in a film you've already encountered, but done better back in the day, hits us this soon. During these credits, a Vatican priest does some serious reading up on the early chapters of his field of expertise: biblical talk of the coming of The Devil; the apocalypse, the rise of evil and the clues it's all going to arrive. Satisfied, he outlines via a lecture for the benefit of his peers, as well as our own, in what is one of the more guilt-edged scenes of exposition teeing things up: that things such as 9/11 and the 2004 Indian Ocean Tsunami allude to the end of the world.Not so far away in Italy, a certain top-end American politician named Richard Thorn (Schreiber) is arriving at a hospital on account of the fact his wife is about to give birth. She does so, but with an irk of fate that sees the child die and Thorn offered, by a shady looking religious man we know better than to trust in films such as these, an immediate replacement in the form of another new-born whose scenario was inverted in that HIS mother died during the birth. Without wife Katherine (Stiles) knowing, and in a snap moral decision, Thorn accepts the proposal thus allowing everybody to go home happy - so to speak. Alas, it is the spawn of The Devil that the Thorns have taken away with them and he has an agenda: an agenda which begins with a post-Final Destination death sequence allowing for Richard to garner status as America's ambassador to Great Britain before going on to end with the taking over of the world after growing into his inherited position of power.Cue a lot of strangeness, shenanigans and running around trying to work out just what on Earth is going on. Most of the better scenes from before are once again depicted, they've just had a slight rejigging to come across as more.... I'm not sure of the word. The kids would probably use the term "epic". Trips to the zoo no longer consist of animals sensing the evil and charging off. Now, we need scenes wherein these huge gorillas, upon witnessing the kid, attempt to bash the life out of the transparent observation panels around them as everyone else screams and run for cover. When Katherine comes to have THAT little accident involving a stepladder and a potted plant, the film feels the need to draw blood from her face, but we've already sort of been taken out of the moment anyway on account of the fact the tricycle has gone and has been replaced by one of those toy scooters that were popular with kids for all of about two months in 2000.On the issue of having the impact dented, anonymity in the casting process has been replaced with familiarity. I'm reminded of the tag line for Lethal Weapon 4: "The gang's all here!" We get Pete Postlethwaite turning up as the English priest who first contacts Thorn over his son's true nature but the audience let off a smile at the fact we identify him rather than become more engrossed at the horror unfolding. We observe Michael Gambon playing a sprawling, semi-mad priest based where Europe meets Asia and nothing quite seems right anyway, and it's enough to take us out of the moment. We get English actor David Thewlis playing the photojournalist cottoning on to what's happening, but because it's the 21st Century he needs to uncover those famous blemishes on his photographs in those famous scenes via an iMac screen. To be fair, the film does maintain that creepy scene from the original wherein a photo, in a darkroom already bathed in red light, gradually comes to life revealing the truth ? only, it's not creepy here. Mia Farrow pops up as the nanny and the film, to its credit, actually makes better use of her than before. Where the abuse of this quasi star system doesn't rear up, however, is in the lead and his wife: two people I'd never before come across and whose plight I almost got caught up in on the BASIS of anonymity. The film is probably the best of what I've seen of Moore's work, and probably better than either of the original's two sequels, but this is somewhat desperate stuff in the long run.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-03-19</date>
		<username>Falconeer</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Vastly inferior, &amp; totally pointless remake of a classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Even if the original film, "The Omen" didn't exist, this sub-par horror effort would be forgettable at best. But because this is a remake of one of the most beautiful, beloved horror classics of all time, the 2006 "Omen" becomes a real chore to sit through. The filmmakers here have managed, while ripping off/paying tribute to a great film, to do just about everything wrong. Starting with the wretched casting. The 1976 film featured Lee Remmick as the wife of the British Ambassader, played to the hilt by Gregory Peck. Remmick and Peck were in their forties, which made sense. But for this brighter, MTV style remake, we have the utterly forgettable Liev Schreiber, and his seemingly "child-bride", Julia Stiles! Preposterous. People in the audience were laughing whenever she was on screen. Yes, Stiles is in her twenties, but she looks like a teenager. In the famous scene where the couple drives to church with Damian, she looks like a little girl playing dress-up in the way too sophisticated outfit, complete with pillbox style hat. It is a mystery why she was cast, as she is also a terrible actress. The rest of the cast was utterly forgettable, delivering their lines, (which were for the most part lifted, word for word from the original), like they were talking in their sleep. With the exception of Mia Farrow as the Nanny, who did manage a strange creepiness. I suppose the makers of this rubbish thought that her name in the credits would lend some much needed legitimacy to this miscarriage of a film. The boy who played Damian was fairly ordinary as well. As for the music? Well, who could forget the chilling Jerry Goldsmith score that graced all three films in the "Omen" trilogy? Powerhouse scores that dripped with evil and religious mystery? What of the score for this remake? I don't know, as I cannot remember it. Whatever music was there was so utterly forgettable, I'm not sure there was any music at all. And the look of the film? Was it a "made for TV" effort? There is not one scene that possesses any atmosphere whatsoever. I waited for my personal favorite scene from the original: the'graveyard scene.' That scene in the ancient, mist-shrouded graveyard, protected by the Rottweillers from hell is one of the most amazingly terrifying scenes in horror film history. The remake managed to ruin this as well. Of course the violence factor is turned up a bit, in an effort to compensate for the countless shortcomings. Yet still, the death scenes in the original were far more effective. The scene where the nanny hangs herself, splatting dully against the wall, rather than crashing through the window, was underwhelming. And the scene that has the priest impaled in the church yard tried so hard to be shocking, but this is a case where less is really more. Although the scene where Miss Baylock, played by Mia Farrow, murders the 'teeny-bopper' mom by injecting an air bubble into her blood tube, topped the original, and was decidedly more sinister than simply having her thrown out of the window. But that was the only scene in the whole film that worked. I implore fans of good cinema, especially quality horror films, to avoid this one and check out the original 1976 film, looking even more majestic in it's 2.35:1 widescreen version, currently available on DVD. I must say I feel a bit sorry for this generation of moviegoers, as what is on offer today is so inferior to the films of the last three decades. Being a fan of the original, i was curious to see this remake, and i went into it with an open mind, after reading many inexplicably good reviews here on IMDb.But I can honestly say, it is not even good enough as a curiosity piece. Stick with the original. And while you are at it, try to hunt down the original DVD of "The Exorcist". Not the amped up "new version," but the original theatrical version, which is hard to find these days. Why can't Hollywood just leave the classic films alone, and come up with something fresh for this generation of movie fans?</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A worthy remake. Nothing new, but not bad.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>My Take: Somewhat unnecessary, but overall enjoyable and rather effective. This is just one of the first horror movies I saw in the big-screen, and it was good. Although not as great as the original, this remake is well crafted. It has a fine atmosphere, great at producing a scare or two. The film is very similar to the original, since writer David Seltzer recycled much of the script and elements from the first hit film. But he does create a thrilling start, about the prophecy, the coming of the Antichrist. It was really interesting and almost as frightening as they wanted it to be. The cast was okay. Julia Stiles was no match for Lee Remick who landed the role of Kathryn Thorn of the original film, but tackles the role finely. It actually was spooky when she told her husband, Robert (Liev Schreiber), in the hospital the lines "don't let him kill." Sure it was said in the original, but it's fun seeing it again and still get a real kick out of it. Then of course, the main course in THE OMEN table is always the death scenes, and while you already had the chance to see (and took it) in the original, the deaths are still gruesomely remade. Although you saw it in the original, it was a bit scarier now with better special-effects. The performances are decent, with Schreiber in fine form in the Gregory Peck role (honestly, a good replacement). Director John Moore stages it much close to the 1976 thriller, but moves in brisk pace and with never lack of thrills and quality suspense. It's a good throwback to an old-fashioned thriller where plot and characters are far more important than setting up a blood bath. This film is indeed gory, but not without being finely plotted, even if does copy everything the original had already done. This remake was really entertaining, despite some bad criticism. It isn't as original as the original horror cult classic, but just to be fair, why compare? Rating: **** out of 5.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>An excellent remake!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I must admit I really enjoyed this remake of the original 'Omen' for numerous reasons. Primarily this is one of those films that really works where the majority of remakes of prior films are ultimately dreadful, as has been evident with so many movies. We pretty much would all agree that remakes rarely even equal the original. In the case of 'The Omen 6.6.06', the result is truly unique. The film works well on virtually every level even though this remake rarely dwells far from the material of the original script. One would think that this very fact might have well doomed this remake to a complete waste of time, money, and celluloid. But here are just few reasons I think this film works and works well.The sets are always impressive as is the art direction through out this movie. The supporting musical score is rich with texture and mood through the entire movie without ever going in the direction of predictable or cheesy. The score is also never over bearing for even a second. I also found it interesting to see that scenes when violent moments take place, the film makers chose to take the direction of down playing the gore and possibly topping the original with predictable and silly over the top blood and carnage. As well as these observations, I felt the film was surprisingly well cast and acted. When first being introduced to the various characters my first feeling was- "Oh boy- here we go. This guy just doesn't fit this role". Well as character development continued I am happy to say I was wrong about this perception, especially in the case of Pete Postlewaite's interpretation of the tormented priest. A superb and better than average actor nicely fit into this role. As did Mia Farrow in her part. I was happy to see a classic veteran actor like Farrow finally having the chance to play an evil character. The only side note about her part is not so much the part it's self but realizing that she is still breathtakingly beautiful despite her elder age, and even the less than appealing makeup, clothing, and hair design couldn't hide that fact. There are some superbly shot dream sequences that add a little more scare factor to the film that were not present in the original movie. All in all I felt that there were moments of the original that make it somehow better than this version, and at the same time there was plenty here in this outing that I frankely felt topped the original. And let's face it, the original isn't always perfect either, especially in the earlier character setups of the film. There is an almost "aw-shucks" kind of mood to some of the early portions of the original and I found myself saying "God how cheesy". Moments such as the interrelative sequences between Gregory Peck and lee Remmick after they move into their new English home. It does get a bit hokey at times- let's face it. And the supporting lighter spirited music at times almost gives those scenes an 'As the world turns' sort of flavor. Another example of this is when Damien is wandering outside with his parents and he disappears. When they find him and realize he's OK the music nearly ruins that scene, it's downright silly. Beyond this though the original is a superb horror classic, and after all it was made a pretty long time ago, so it get's a pass for the few and far between weaker moments. So basically I feel that this remake is a solid movie with a great deal of detail, creepiness, and style. it's worth seeing and I think it's much better than many horror movies made over the past 20 years.</fullReview>

		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>This unnecessary remake adds nothing new.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Whilst not an exact carbon-copy, this remake of the classic 70s horror plays it safe, preferring to stay very faithful to the original plot, rather than to attempt anything radically different. For those familiar with Richard Donner's superb '76 movie, this updated version (release date 6/6/06?very clever!) probably seems like an unnecessary endeavour. Those new to the tale will undoubtedly find much to enjoy, such is the strength of the story, but they would be well advised to seek out the far superior original.This time around, Liev Schrieber takes the role of Robert Thorne (played in the original by Gregory Peck), the American politician who unwittingly adopts Damien, the son of the Devil, and Julia Stiles plays Katherine, his wife, who gradually suspects that the child isn't quite who he seems. And, unfortunately, I lay a large portion of the blame for this film's lack of potency squarely at the feet of Mr. Schrieber and Ms. Stiles. Both lack the necessary acting chops to provide the conviction that the roles require; rather than conveying absolute terror at their predicament, they only ever manage to look 'very concerned'.Director John Moore and writer David Seltzer must share the rest of the blame. The few tweaks that Seltzer has made to the original story are mostly unnecessary padding and Moore weakens the film by replacing the original's foreboding sense of menace with cheap scares.On the plus side, the celebrated death scenes from Donner's movie are convincingly recreated, and the most famous (the gory decapitation) is given a nice new twist. The supporting cast, which comprises of much better actors than the two leads, give great performances; Mia Farrow is wonderfully menacing as Damien's nanny Mrs. Baylock, David Thewlis gives a nice turn as doomed photographer Jennings and Pete Postlethwaite is impressive as Father Brennan.After viewing the movie, I was left asking myself two questions: a) why would anyone choose to watch this over the original (other than out of curiosity)? and b) is it common practice in Rome to brake fuel tankers parked on hills using only a wooden block pushed behind a wheel?</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>As in, "Oh man, here's yet ANOTHER horror remake..." (Well, I paid for the ticket, so the least I can do is write a review in penance for my misguided selection.) It seemed like a really good idea--take the somewhat dated (yet mostly well-regarded) horror film "The Omen" and make it relevant to a 21st-Century audience. Then again, I suppose that uninspired line is what all big-studio execs use to justify what is really, in the end, simply a ploy to milk dollars from a household name. Like "The Exorcist," "The Omen" is a road-tested horror title that blends Catholicism with life in the public sector and devilish mischief. From what I can remember, the original film combined an intriguing plot with some uneven pacing and indifferent acting (mostly from Gregory Peck, who makes Keanu Reeves look like Olivier), plus a few fine kills. Therefore, I imagined the potential for improvement that could come from this effort.Unfortunately, the success rate of horror remakes within the last five years has been none-too-surprisingly low...if it ain't broke (and it usually ain't), then why go to the trouble? Well, if nothing else, there is a market of uninformed cineasts and curiosity seekers (-raises one guilty hand-) chomping at the bit for the latest slice of gore-greased titillation. In the case of "The Omen," this immense group provides a chance to run off with a bandit's ransom before negative word-of-mouth poisons the box office.This year's remake of "The Hills Have Eyes" did the genre proper--with a director who had proved his horror mettle (with "High Tension"), the template of Wes Craven's original was given not only a superficial modern face-lift, but the film itself crept along with a genuine sense of dread and suspense, tweaking the original story into an innovative new tale. The remake of "The Omen" is a film with similar potential, but becomes increasingly lopsided the further it progresses as the result of some of the central ingredients crucial to any good film.Top-billed Julia Stiles, known primarily for "A Million Other Teen Movies," has the most difficulty with the material--as Catherine Thorn, she emotes and speaks so self-consciously that it transforms her character into an automaton for whom we feel nihil. Liev Schreiber ("The Manchurian Candidate") fares better in the role of Ambassador Robert Thorn, and seems to be channeling the indifference of Gregory Peck, 20 years younger; surprisingly, his detachment works so well that his brief attempts at showing emotion wreck the characterization. In a bit of stunt casting, Mia Farrow plays the nanny, Mrs. Baylock, in such an overtly sinister manner that she invokes Jack Nicholson's out-the-gate insanity in "The Shining." Italian horror fave Giovanni Lombardo Radice ("The Gates of Hell"; "Cannibal Ferox") has a dialog-free scene as a severely scarred priest, and conveys more horror than anything else in the film. David Thewlis takes over the David Warner role, and brings a distinct "Island of Dr. Moreau" sensibility to his performance.Come to think of it, "The Omen"'s central problem may be the way each of its actors seems to be performing in a completely different film. The interactions and dialog scenes are always uncharacteristically off-center, to the point of distraction. John Moore, who directed the film under the duress of meeting its gimmicky release date (6/6/06, isn't that a scream?), seems to have marginalized the actors (who still had the original film as a reference) in favor of recycled technical conventions intended to provoke that effect known as...(yawn)..."shock." Unfortunately, another important area where the film fails is in creating suspense. Save for one or two out-of-left-field "jump" scenes, the horror of this "Omen" is always telegraphed well in advance, to the point where one can sharpshoot a scare from a mile away (the balcony scene is a prime example of this). The film is adrift in too many instances of terror-orgasm-diffusion. There is a frustrating lack of risk in the production as a whole that makes the experience maddening; too afraid of doing something new, we're left with the continual reminder of how good yesteryear's leftovers tasted.I recalled Gus Van Sant's "Psycho" remake with unsurprising frequency during my viewing of "The Omen." In that 1998 stunt (which others have claimed started this whole wave of rotten remakes), a director with marginal talent (at best) tried to outdo (or at least equal) Hitchcock's original with a shot-for-shot retelling. Granted, we got some arbitrary flash-cuts of macabre images during the murders and lonely Norman Bates pounding one out to Marion Crane, in addition to some amped-up gore. Even though I didn't loathe the film as much as the world at large, it did little except draw attention to Van Sant's glaring limitations as a filmmaker. Really, what is the point in remaking something if you're going to leave it relatively unchanged? "The Omen" is a similar stunt that carries a draining, time-wasting effect. Augmenting the timely release date, the film's prologue includes a Vatican-sponsored montage of current disasters (9/11, Hurricane Katrina, etc.) that do, for the sake of this movie, point to the Antichrist's imminent arrival! Save for this bit of armchair prophecy, the film feels relatively unchanged from its source--sure, it looks more slick and employs modern technology (digital cameras! guns with laser-sights!), but that's no excuse for sinking $60 million into something like this. Nor is the promise of murders so shallowly extravagant they inspire bad laughs (watch for the photographer's 'break dance' down some stairs--you won't miss it) and make one long for the days when fear wasn't measured by buckets of Karo Syrup and red dye.If "The Omen" signals the end times for anything, let's hope it's this rash of needless remakes. Damien, you've infiltrated Hollywood with this dire flick...it's time to finish the job!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>One of the better recent retreads of 1970's horror films.Okay you know the drill The Thorn baby dies in child birth so in order to protect the mother's feelings the father agrees to substitute another newborn, little knowing that the child is literally hell-spawn and will leave a trail of death in his wake.Well made and mostly well acted this is remake of one of the touch stones of 1970's film. These flaws were in the original but Gregory Peck sucked you in and you never noticed how stupid it all was. It doesn't help that it may be a bit too close to the original for its own good. Its been awhile but it seems that they may have cribbed a bit too much from the original. 		<shortDescription>Surprisingly good remake! **** out of 5</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Omen has always been one of my favorite horror films so of course I'm going to be somewhat hesitant on seeing a remake of such. Secretly though for years I had always fantasized about how I would remake the film and add little touches here and there. Nothing that would improve upon the original but just things I felt would go well with a retelling.Thankfully this film is pretty much the route I would have gone with. Many people have complained about how it is so much like the original. Personally, I felt even though the story structure and most of the death scenes were alike, it wasn't shot by shot the same film (like the Psycho remake). It went in it's own direction.I'm glad David Seltzer, writer of the original, came back to spruce up things.The kid who plays Damien is absolutely fantastic. Cold and emotionless he had an aura about him that certainly felt evil. Liev Schreiber is great as always. By far one of the greatest and most underrated actors today in movies. And I loved the appearances by Pete Postlethwaite, Michael Gambon, and Mia Farrow.I was bowled over with excitement that Marco Beltrami was on board to score the film. To me he has been the Jerry Goldmsith of the 21st Century and it was only appropriate that he score the remake of a film Goldsmith had scored years ago. He does a great job at doing his own thing and only hints at the themes that were used in the original. It's not a landmark score and of course doesn't surpass the classic original, but it's still a non-conventional, appropriate and effective score.There are nuances and subliminalities here and there which I appreciated. A key moment I loved was when Stiles is in the bathtub and sees her 'son' watching her. What happens during this scene will really disturb you. The scenes that were best recreated in my opinion were the 'falling over the balcony' scene where Stiles is pushed over by Damien and the cemetery scene where the dogs attack. There was an unexpected moment of humor before the infamous 'nanny hanging' scene where not just everyone is staring but also two Punch and Judy puppets.It's directed well, atmospheric, and even emotional. It may not be a classic, but no one should have ever expected that. This is one of the remakes that succeeds.</fullReview>
