		<shortDescription>He's a Devil of Son !</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Omen has been dragged kicking and screaming into the 21st century, and the remake is not bad considering everything. There is a clever move to lock it into the 6/6/06 date, with a new prologue, and a general modernization of the story accordingly. The ingenious plot concerns a US Ambassador Robert Thorn whose "son" seems to cause some horrific deaths, and may be a Satanist child, the Antichrist as prophesied in Revelations.It remains faithful by and large to the novel and the first movie that starred Gregory Peck. If it is less sharp in its pace, it adds a couple of shock cuts that work well. So while we might miss the tighter direction of Richard Donner, the effects are very good and the photography and editing also of a high standard with some suitably exotic locations.Though Leiv Schrieber lacks the gravitas of Gregory Peck, who was older in the part at the end of his distinguished career, (though not his finest performance) Schrieber manages to deliver his lines with something of a resonance of Peck. Less satisfactory is Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick as Damien, who lacks the evil eye of the original's Harvey Stephens. While it was neat idea to have Mia Farrow as the malevolent governess Mrs. Baylock, (a reference to Rosemary's Baby which covered similar ground), her first appearance rather suggests Mary Poppins, and it seems hard for her to then make the role as threatening as did Billie Whitelaw.Neither do Julia Stiles as Karen (the long suffering wife) nor David Thewlis as the photographer Keith Jennings improve on the performances of Lee Remick and David Warner. However both Postlethwaite and Gamon provide some strong characterization. You may well wonder why it took so long for Thorn to wake up about his daemonic son, you'd only have to look at the drawings by the kid pasted on the nursery walls of all places, to realize the lad has some serious problems.In the early 1970s there was a sudden interest in the Devil and all his works, with films like Rosemary's Baby, The Exorcist, The Omen, etc. mostly they were well made and with good casts and directors, its hard to improve on the originals even with today's film technology. So why do we bother remaking big features from the past, perhaps so a new generation can see them, but usually the revival is lacking the spark that set off the original idea, and sometimes the story has simply become old hat - just as Poseidon really sank itself the second time round. However in fairness The Omen 2006 is well worth a viewing, if you haven't seen the first version.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A fabulously scary remake...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I had trepidation about seeing a modern remake of such a masterpiece of horror as the original 1976 The Omen, directed by Dick Donner, especially when the new version was cast with much younger actors. I have to say, however, that the end result was not only chillingly scary but even more believable, especially for a modern audience.For example, Mia Farrow offered a less cliché minion-of-Hell-in-nanny's-clothing than the original Mrs. Baylock. Did anyone else notice that the black dog in the beginning (when the original nanny hung herself) was not the Rottweiler used later in the movie? My memory of the original isn't perfect but I always thought it was the same dog throughout.All told, The Omen 2006, has stayed true and done justice to the original, and thanks to great direction and acting, has the legs to stand on its own as a classic of the genre.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Liev Schreiber was part of a very important horror movie. Ten years ago, he was in "Scream", a movie that made horror funny. And now, today, he's done it again. The Omen is a movie that takes itself SO seriously, it becomes a parody of itself halfway through. Disastrously po-faced, John Moore is so amazed at how awesome his director of photography is, he jump cuts, steadicams and desaturates as fast as he can. But while the original movie built up a claustrophobic dread - a slow build to the awful truths that lay along the way, the 2006 cover version does away with that - that might require talent. Instead, it takes the main set pieces and links them together with lumpen dialogue, sledgehammer symbolism and dreadful music. The uneasiness you feel in the modern Omen is down to not knowing when the next cheap scare will be - a music sting from nowhere, or a flash of an image that has no relevance to the movie, but, y'know - LOOKS EVIL.If Hollywood insists on continually remaking good movies, can you at least do it properly? I mean, how hard is it to do the Omen well? this film is yet another example of how mentally bankrupt some studios have become.It gets two stars for making me laugh out loud in several places. 		<username>w00f</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>A Snoozefest Punctuated by Cheap Shots</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In all honesty, I was never a fan of the original Omen movies. I found them generally boring, ineffective, and flat all the way around. Still, when I started seeing ads for the remake I though that maybe ? just maybe ? with all the new technology available and a little re-writing, this could have been turned into something worth seeing.It wasn't. Oh, how it wasn't.As for the ads, those flashes you see in the trailers and TV commercials are all that you'll see of those images in the movie. In fact, all of those scary moments are inserted into the story as dream sequences. The rest of the film plods along so lamely that by the time something marginally exciting happens, you'll be too deeply asleep to care.Lead actor Liev Schreiber managed to get through almost the entire film without displaying a trace of emotion. He spends most of his on-screen time staring straight ahead and reciting his lines in a monotone sure to induce drowsiness in even the most caffeine-sodden insomniac. Still, his performance is better than that of child actor Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick who barely has any lines at all and generally mugs and looks pouty for the camera. Julia Stiles is a bit better and Mia Farrow actually manages to be simultaneously frail and menacing in spots. The rest of the cast is just window dressing, really, and their roles are formulaic.There is one realistic-looking beheading, three or four dog attacks, and a priest-kabob that isn't nearly as effective as director John Moore apparently thinks it is (and when did newspapers start running gory images of accident victims on page 1, anyhow?) The camera work itself alternates between a seemingly endless fixed shot and sudden nauseating vibrations that take place presumably to try and insert feeling into scenes that threaten to develop frostbite under Liev Schreiber's icy, lifeless stare.All in all, this is pretty weak stuff. If this is the best the son of the devil can do, we don't have much to worry about.</fullReview>

		<username>nycityactorguy</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Not Worth It</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The original OMEN is one of my favorite horror films and I have to admit I was pretty excited to see the remake. In retrospect, this was a bit foolish of me considering practically every remake Hollywood's perpetrated in the last 10 years has been god-awful (with the possible exception of Manchurian Candidate). This one is equally unfortunate, blending unnecessarily flashy camera-work, an over-the-top soundtrack, mediocre acting, and just plain lazy storytelling. Liev Schreiber is a fine actor and does his best here, but you can't help but feel he's "phoning it in", especially during his repeated episodes of blubbering; one longs for the gravitas of Gregory Peck. I've never been a fan of Julia Stiles and she is woefully miscast here, lending more of an amateurish quality to the film. The supporting cast fares better with Mia Farrow (in a bit of stunt casting that actually works) as the creepy nanny, and reliable British actors David Thewlis, Pete Postlethwaite, and Michael Gambon chewing the scenery. I couldn't quite figure out why elements that worked so well in the original film played out so ineffectively here, almost laughable. I think that a lot of it actually had to do with young Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick, who plays the little devil child. In the original film, Harvey Stephens was creepy without even trying. This performance tries very hard to affect us and fails. There are a few unfortunate shots of Damien where it looks like the actor's been directed to look really constipated. I didn't buy it for a second and I think it added to the film's overall schlockiness. The original OMEN is an effective thriller and succeeded in telling this story perfectly. I guess I've learned my lessons for future remakes.</fullReview>

		<username>rixatrix</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>True to the original with some surprises of its own</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw this movie opening night, because I'm such a big fan of the original. The story-line was true to the original movie, and in fact this remake uses the same scriptwriter as the first. The cast is great--Mia Farrow (Rosemary's Baby) plays the demonic nanny, Michael Gambon (Dumbledore in the newer Harry Potter movies) reprises his role as Bugenhagen, David Thewlis (Professor Lupin in Harry Potter) plays the prophetic photographer--and these are all secondary roles! As for the movie itself, it was beautifully shot. The use of color to set mood is even more pronounced than in the original version. The movie's timing was spot on, and even though I've seen The Omen countless times before, I still found myself jumping in my seat at times. Unlike many movies today, the shock of being taken by surprise is very well done here. I also enjoyed the special effects, especially surrounding the death scenes.All in all, I was so taken by this remake that I'm planning to see it again very soon. I was absolutely delighted by this movie, because it succeeds where so many other horror/suspense movies fail--it's clever, cinematographically beautiful, has brilliant timing, thoughtful acting, and doesn't rely on too much blood or cheap tricks to carry the story. Any fan of the first movie will be a fan of the remake, as will any film geek, and many more people who are seeing the movie for the first time. Enjoy.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Good Omen</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I always loved the original so I didn't set my expectations too high for this film. Inspired merely by a spur of the moment thing (it was the 6th of the 6th of the 6th after all so what better thing to do than watch The Omen) I can say that I was very pleasantly surprised...The acting, the atmosphere, the chills, the thrills and the outstanding effects were spot on and I don't think I could really find a fault in any it (or if there is then I was so engrossed by the whole thing that it passed me by)... The whole story, whether the original or, now, the remake is very tightly packaged. And what of the actors? Well Liev Schreiber has a major problem in that Robert Thorn, in the original, was played by the much loved actor Gregory Peck. And because of this Schreiber will always be compared to the legendary actor. But Schreiber manages to act up a little storm all of his very own. I found him convincing enough to pull off the role.Mia Farrow is superbly cast as nanny, Mrs Baylock. She plays the role a little nicer than her predecessor which makes the role that much more unsettling for us as an audience when she... Well, I'll leave you to see the film. Together she and Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick (Damien) build up an excellent screen rapport and create a somewhat unsettling chemistry.David Thewlis was one of my highlights though as he provides excellent support to Liev Schreiber and managed to convince me in his role.I'm not saying the original isn't better because it is. But this film has a ballsy try at approaching a much loved classic horror from a new angle and makes it much more contemporary so as to fit in with todays audiences.The deaths are far more graphic and, in my opinion, work a lot better. There are also plenty of "jump-out-of-your-skin" moments none of which I will mention here.Although the original is a better film on the whole there is still in places with the remake a greater feeling of dread and the whole thing just seems much more plausible and much closer to hand thanks to the use of linking key news events to passages in the Bible. In short, I found the remake considerably more unsettling and scary than its original predecessor.The general feel from the audience was that it was a very enjoyable film (and the morning after I can still feel the palpitations my heart were making last night watching the film)...I would greatly recommend this film for lovers of the original and for people new to The Omen films (although, is there anyone out there who's never seen The Omen - I mean, come on! It's a classic!)...I can quite honestly say that I have never been so thrilled or close to the point of terror as I felt when watching this film - maybe that's just me, though.However, when it comes to films, and especially horror films, I'm usually VERY difficult to please (being more of a theatrical person). But The Omen manages to hit all the right notes for me. It is just so incredible how Moore has managed to bring to the screen this new take on a much loved and respected classic bringing many new additions to it whilst at the same time managing to be very faithful to the original.The only thing that I would suggest is to go and see it with an open mind. In other words don't be thinking it's going to be as good as the original because it simply isn't. There are too many die hard fans of the original commenting on this film saying that it's not that good - well, it was never intended to surpass the original, was it? Surely it was meant as a means to introduce this film to a new audience.However, in it's own right this is a very good try at recreating a film that will haunt you for days and maybe weeks afterwards. And in an age of film where remakes just aren't hitting the spot this is a welcome addition proving that a remake doesn't have to mean an inferior film. It is pretty much on the same par as the original so GO AND SEE IT!!! And let's just hope that they remake the other two, and far inferior, chapters. If they do then I'll be first in line.</fullReview>

		<date>2006-06-06</date>
		<username>jsnyder-15</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Hey honey, how does the name Damien sound??</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm afraid to say it but here goes ? the horror genre has seen its day. There was a time when films like "Halloween" and "The Exorcist" were taken seriously, before Hollywood threw mindless sequel after sequel at us. One of these movies that helped define the genre was "The Omen", a movie about a child who is the son of the Devil, sent to Earth to start the apocalypse. Released in 1976 "Omen" ranks up there with the best of them as a gripping, unforgettable thriller.Thirty years later (and on June 6th, 2006 I might add) director John Moore (Behind Enemy Lines, Flight of the Phoenix) brings us a remake of said film in an attempt to breath some life back into the genre. This time around we have Leiv Schreiber (The Manchurian Candidate) and Julia Stiles (The Bourne Identity) playing Robert and Katherine Thorn, the loving parents of Damien (Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick) who may or may not be the son of the Devil. If you're a fan of the original you will be glad to hear that this "Omen" is a very faithful remake, treating the source material with respect and dignity. In fact, if you've seen the original, you'll pretty much know what will happen in every single scene of this film. Still, Moore is able to throw in a good thrill every once in a while to keep you on your toes.The cast and crew have done a respectable job with the film this time around. Leiv does a great job and carries his role well while Seamus is just downright creepy as Damien. The only one that stumbles is Stiles whose acting is a little stiff in some scenes. It was neat to see Mia Farrow ("Rosemary's Baby") in the role of Mrs. Baylock, Damien's creepy nanny. Seeing her was a nice touch and a throwback to the better days of horror.Despite being faithful there are still some differences. The remake modernizes the original, which changes absolutely nothing in regards to plot. Now everyone has a cell phone, digital camera, and a computer instead of a newspaper and one of those ancient land line telephones. This gives it a different feel but it definitely doesn't feel out of place. The story seems to fit in rather well in today's world (as did the original for that matter). The remake does cut down of some of the "cheese factor" that plagued horror films of the 70s but it comes at a price in the form of the pacing. It's not terrible but it does drag in some spots. What make the pacing problems easier to get around are the set designs and the cinematography. The sets are neat and simple without being too bare, the colors are vivid, and the actors fit right in with their surroundings. "Omen" definitely looks nice which is a big improvement over the sometimes drab original.I can say with certainty that you will like this version just as much as you liked the one thirty years ago. But I can't help but wonder if this remake was needed at all. With so little changed it is essentially the same story told thirty years ago but in today's world. "Omen" is very faithful, for better or worse.Bottom Line: Only die-hard fans of the original or those who've never seen it will appreciate this remake.Final Score: 7 out of 10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Unnecessary, but not abominable</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As has been said many times, the original Omen relied on being a complete shock and surprise to the audience. We questioned everything we saw and could never predict the next frame. It constantly kept you on edge. This film never achieves the same level of tension because it is essentially a shot by shot remake of the first Omen. We're ten steps ahead of the actors every step of the way. Every time I saw David Thewlis, all I could think was "You're going to be short a head by the end of this film." The new Thorne's are adequate but greatly suffer by comparison to Gregory Peck and Lee Remick. They're too young and just don't have the gravitas of their predecessors. The new Damien is comically evil, which completely eliminates the shock and ambivalence we felt in the original when Thorn actually took steps towards killing him. There's no interior struggle in the audience whatsoever because this kid is obviously evil and needs to die. Mrs. Baylock is played rather hammily by Mia Farrow and becomes an audience favorite instead of the leering, frightening mad dog she was in the original. Her death in the new film is one of the few things that is actually less gory than the original. Which brings me to what this film does right. Unfortunately, all that justifies this film is two deaths: Father Brennan's death is actually a lot more effective in this one than it was in the original and Jennings beheading, though not quite as iconic as it was in the original due in large part to its being expected, is very well done. The film is also very visually lush: there's definitely a far larger budget at work than that of the original. But it's missing the music (which was half of what made the original frightening) and just the overall spirit of the 1978 version.</fullReview>

		<date>2010-02-01</date>
		<username>Paul Andrews (poolandrews@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Decent but somewhat inferior remake of a classic.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Omen starts in Rome as Katherine Thorn (Julia Stiles) the wife of Deputy US Ambassador Robert Thorn (Liev Schreiber) gives birth, however Robert is informed that the newborn baby has died &amp; that they are willing to substitute another baby whose mother has died which Robert agrees to. Several years later &amp; Robert's boss the US Ambassador Steven Haines dies in a tragic accident &amp; Robert inherits his job, Robert is then placed as the US Ambassdor to Britain &amp; moves there with his wife Katherine &amp; young son Damien. At first all goes well but after their nanny hangs herself things go from bad to worse as an old priest (Pete Postlethwaite) warns Robert that Damien is the son of the Devil &amp; that he is in danger, as more people die under mysterious circumstances &amp; the things the priest said start to make more &amp; more sense Robert has to accept that he must kill his own son but the forces of darkness are at work &amp; it won't be as easy as that...Directed by John Moore this Hollywood remake of the classic demonic horror film The Omen (1976) follows a long line of recent horror remakes including The Texas Chainsaw Massacre (2003), The Fog (2005), The Hills Have Eyes (2006) &amp; Halloween (2007) to name but four &amp; while some may have an intense dislike of remake by their very nature I don't have a problem with them as the original films still exist anyway. Here with The Omen the makers have hired David Seltzer to write the script who actually wrote the original way back in the 70's although the original director Richard Donner wasn't invited back, to be honest Seltzer doesn't stray too far away from his original script &amp; this remake follows the original very closely &amp; sometimes even word for word. All of the major incidents &amp; plot points are here, from the death's to Damien's birthmark to the priest who tries to warn Robert to Katherines fall to the switching of babies to the evil dogs to the sinister nanny to the exact same ending as Damien appears to have been adopted by the US President. To be fair The Omen makes for a decent enough horror thriller with political overtones (it's implied that politicians are the real Satan &amp; that they are the real evil in our society rather than ancient belief's) that isn't too deep but does have enough dramatic moments to lift it above the usual one tone teen slashers. At almost two hours long it does drag a little at times but there's just about enough going on to sustain ones attention &amp; interest, overall &amp; good effort that isn't amazing or even as good as the original but worth watching especially if you haven't seen the original &amp; don't fancy watching a film 25 years old (which I know some don't).In a neat publicity move the film was released on 06/06/06 &amp; one has to wonder whether part of the reason this was ever made was for that unique marketing gimmick. Apparently director Moore used the colour red to signify danger although I must admit I didn't really notice it myself. There's a few gory moments, someone is impaled &amp; there's a really cool decapitation &amp; the set-pieces are predictably bigger than the originals with CGI &amp; more scale. There's a nice visual style here too, there's some stylish &amp; striking sets &amp; the film looks nice throughout but that can lead to it looking a bit staged at times.With a supposed budget of about $25,000,000 this wasn't a mega budget production, I think it could be described as modest. Shot largely in Prague in the Czech Republic with some filming in Dublin &amp; Italy &amp; even though this is set in Britain you can see the odd sign written in Czech. Apparently Pierce Brosnan &amp; Jim Carey were considered for the role of Robert while Rachel Weisz turned the part of Katherine down because she was pregnant. Harvey Stephens who played Damien in the 1976 original has a small cameo as a newspaper reporter while the atmospheric teaser trailer was actually Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick's screen test.The Omen is a fine remake of a classic horror film, it follows the original very closely &amp; anyone familiar with the original will have a strong feeling of déj? vu, for those of you that haven't seen the original this is worth a watch as it's slightly better than average.</fullReview>
	</review>

Especially when he agrees to substitute this child with his own that gets murdered in a conspiracy of devils, and doesn't even try to get help from his wealthy family or anyone official. He knows he screwed up, and he takes the burden of trying to save mankind from the folly of his own ways.What bothered me is the many many people who kept claiming that this was a shot for shot remake, and it was a lie. While some elements of the 1976 original remain intact to pay homage to the writer and the work of those involved, it is not the same movie. There are changes to the story to make it more like today, and you get the sense that if you didn't know any better you would swear that it was actually real and not a movie. Horror movies are usually all fantasy, blood, guts, and gore. This one reels you in, makes you think and feel, and it won't let you go.Unfortunately, the Thorns had zero chemistry. No question. They are married but they act like old pals who have lost that special magic, and that doesn't hold a candle to Gregory Peck and Lee Remick. Neither one of them have any chemistry to Damien either. It's like the lights were on, but no one is home. It reminds me of one of those mediocre school plays from yesteryear where everyone does their part perfectly, but there is no passion and no feeling. In the movie, a lot of bad things are happening but no one cares. This from the one man who thinks he is going to save us all from little Damien and his real Daddy.Besides the poor acting from the main characters, I did enjoy Mia Farrow who played Mrs. Baylock. She stood way out, and looked like she had fun doing it.The rest of the movie was outstanding. If you are looking for a great story and you need a good scare at the same time, this one is for you.</fullReview>

		<date>2009-06-28</date>
		<username>sgtking</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Needless, but surprisingly solid update of the classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Nowadays it seems like every film that left a mark on the world and was a hit at the time it was released is being remade for the current generation. The problem is that in some cases the sole purpose for doing so seems to be making lots of money. The first, though not the first period, major remake was 1998s new version of 'Psycho.' But instead of an interesting new spin on the original story, what we got was a shot-for-shot, word-for-word retread of the classic set in the present. What is the point of that I ask? Fortunately director Gus Van Sant has proved his worth since then with films like 'Elephant' and 'Milk.' The ones that have come since turned out to be less a remake and more a reinvention of the original stories. 30 years after the original's release, 20th Century Fox unleashed a modern version of their hit 'The Omen' and unfortunately like that remake of Alfred Hitchcock's film there wasn't much different between this film and it's original. However, some of the differences there are and the talent in front of and behind the camera mostly make up for that and make this new 'Omen' film much better than some other horror remakes and than it deserves to be.Pros: Good work by Liev Schrieber and the supporting cast. Beautifully photographed. After a slow start this film moves at an effortless pace. A good score, which includes a decent new version of the original theme. Exquisite architecture. Awesome death scenes/set pieces. Some of the changes and new additions to the script are welcome. Like the original, this film has a dread-filled gloomy tone. Some good scares.Cons: If you've seen the original you'll find few surprises here. The talented Julia Stiles is woefully miscast here. I'll give her this though, she does get better as the film goes along. The dream sequences, which I'm sure were meant to be scary, are silly and stick out like a sore thumb. The actor playing the new Damien does fine, but unlike the original's young star he's too obviously evil.Final thoughts: Granted this update of 'The Omen' isn't as effective as the original and it really doesn't add much new to the proceedings, it's still a very well done film on most fronts. Just don't compare it to the original, which it pales in comparison to. On it's own it is quite good and definitely far better than the ill-conceived 'Omen IV: The Awakening.' My rating: 4/5</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2006-06-21</date>
		<username>cam26</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>what a remake is supposed to be</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>First let me say that I'm a fan of the original, and, apparently, so are the folks behind this remake, because, for once, Hollywood didn't manage to screw it up. (See my review of "The Fog" remake for an example of "screwing it up") I think what I liked most was that they treated the original with respect and they took the story seriously. I'm so sick of these tongue-in-cheek, nudge-nudge wink-wink, self-referential horror movies. ("Scream" is a great film, but it's got a lot to answer for). The cast played it straight, and I appreciated that.The script follows the original story pretty closely, with a few minor changes and some nicely done "jump" scares. (I'll admit, they got me a couple of times) True, the score isn't exactly Jerry Goldsmith's classic "Ave Satana", but I thought Marco Beltrami did a decent, if slightly conventional, job.As for the cast : Liev Shrieber is perfect. I thought he brought a much more human, much more emotional Robert Thorne to the screen. Nothing against Gregory Peck, mind you. I actually preferred Julia Stiles' Katherine Thorne to Lee Remick's. She seemed a little more innocent and less of a, well, a bitch, frankly.Awesome supporting cast. Mia Farrow obviously took a completely different take on the Nanny role than Billie Whitelaw. Not better necessarily, just different. David Thewlis was terrific in the Photographer role. Neat accent, too.Do I detect North country? Yorkshire, perhaps? Michael Gambon is pretty much wasted as "Bugenhagen", but then, so was Leo McKern in the original. Pete Postlethwaite's "Father Brennan" is pretty much a carbon copy of Patrick Troughton in the original, and all the better for it. Finally, it's nice to see Italian genre fave Giovanni Lombardo Radice (aka John Morghen)back on screen as "Father Spiletto". (Fantastic burn make-up on him as well. Totally creepy.) Anyways, all in all I thought this was a very well-done remake that should please fans of the original as well as newcomers. Nice job, guys.</fullReview>

		<username>carlrittenhouse</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Attention to detail in The Omen goes under the radar</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'll warn before the spoilers come:The 2006 "The Omen" has generally disappointed critics around the world. However, every review I've seen makes no mention of the attention to detail and organized symbolism. The Omen is an underrated movie, and it seems that almost no one quite understands it. If you haven't seen it yet, watch it, bring your brain, and you'll enjoy yourself. It isn't important to see the 1976 version, this movie is good enough to stand alone (I didn't even know it was a remake until after I watched the 2006 version).The mother wears white for the entire movie, and not surprisingly, she represents goodness and purity. The son, Damien (a name rooted in the word "Devil", but you knew that) almost always is wearing some sort of red on his body, and represents evil. And the father, always seen in black, represents the middle ground between the two. HERE COME SPOILERS: In this movie, red equals evil. Dogs are also a motif, usually representing evil as well. Damien's birthday party is littered with red balloons, and a calm doberman inhabits the background. The evil nanny has red hair, and Damien's room is not only completely drenched in red, but there are sixes all over the wallpaper. In the most dramatic scene of the movie, the nanny is seen gorging Damien with (the reddest of) strawberries. Then, with the red juice all over his face, Damien scooters through the whitest of hallways and bumps his white-clad mother (reaching to water some red roses) off the balcony. In another dramatic scene, the white-clad mother lies in a hospital bed, but is surrounded by red objects, the starkest being the red blanket at her feet, which almost seems to slowly climb over her body as the scene progresses. Then, of course, the nanny comes in and kills her.In the end, the father if forced to choose between supporting the mother or the son. The father is slowly won over by the good, and chooses to kill the evil son. But, as is expected, the son survives, the father dies, and all that is left is the evil, unchecked to ravage the world.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Cannibully</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A worthy remake.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Before I get into my review of this movie, let's talk a bit about remakes. This movie automatically has the odds stacked against it because it is, in fact, a remake. Everybody seems to be complaining that there is a current trend in Hollywood with remaking old movies and TV shows. But think about it, this is not a trend at all. Remakes have been around since the dawn of time. Did you know that The Wizard of Oz and the quote/unquote "original" Frankenstein were both remakes? Hell, The Blue Lagoon starring Brooke Shields was not only a remake, but it was actually the second time that it was remade (see: 1923 and 1949). The bottom line? Remakes have always been around and always will be. Sometimes it works, sometimes it doesn't. Get over it.Now let's address the biggest complaint that you usually hear about remakes. "It isn't faithful to the original." Boo hoo. It's new. Enjoy it for what it is. If you want the original, go watch the original. The remake did not "ruin" the original, as people love to say. The original is still the same movie it always was and always will be.The Omen dares to be different. It actually is faithful to the original. But now, everyone is complaining about that! "It's too much like the original." "It's predictable." "WAAAH WAAAAH." That's right. The same people that complain about remakes being different are now complaining about this being too similar. No matter what you give them they are going to cry and complain. Yet they can't seem to stop themselves from shelling out exorbitant amounts of money to go see these movies that they wish had not been made. Makes sense.But I digress. I believe I was supposed to actually talk about the movie here. This is a good movie, people. Yes, it was predictable because I've seen the original and had a pretty good idea what was going to happen. There were some welcome changes. There were many more "jump-out-of-your-seat" scary moments in this movie. Just when I would find myself absorbed in the story something completely unexpected would happen and scare me nearly to death. This made the movie fun. God forbid you should have fun while watching a movie! The cast in the remake was definitely a good thing. Liev Schreiber delivers his usual solid performance as Robert Thorn. I had my reservations about Julia Styles being cast in a movie of this nature. But, she was surprisingly good. She is quite convincing as a distraught young mother who knows there is something not quite right about her child. Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick is the perfect Damien. I like him better than the original. He manages to be cute as a button and scary as hell at the same time. Mia Farrow's Mrs. Baylock is quite different from the original. This is another welcome change. This Mrs. Baylock is actually likable. She is friendly, lighthearted and pure evil. Her performance is reminiscent of the devil himself. He is not going to show up looking all creepy and scary. He approaches you with a smile and tricks you into trusting him. That's Mia Farrow's Mrs. Baylock. I have to add that Mia Farrow is one lady who has definitely aged gracefully. When she was young she was kind of skinny, gaunt and sickly looking. She is absolutely beautiful as an older woman.But anyway, The Omen is definitely a good movie worth seeing. It is very faithful to the original while adding a few minor and welcome changes. Forget whatever you might think about it being a remake. Just go see it and enjoy yourself for two hours.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2006-06-06</date>
		<username>toshiko79</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A Most Impressive Remake...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Well, today June 6, 2006 or 6/6/06 marked the opening of "The Omen" remake. I saw it earlier this afternoon and I must say, I was quite impressed. Now, we're all aware of Hollywood's obsession to remake everything these days. Most are trite, unremarkable, or simply unnecessary. Some are just plain awful, especially when it comes to modern redos of the classic horror movies (like "The Amityville Horror" remake in 2005) and of course movies based on old TV shows. I have been begging Hollywood for something new and original for almost a decade, but it seems that for now, its just going to be remake after remake. "The Omen" however surprised me. I'm a fan of the original film and its sequels. I had seen remake previews on entertainment shows like "Access Hollywood" and "Extra," so from what I had seen I knew it followed the original film to some extent. However, upon seeing the movie today, it proved to be one of the better remake films I had seen in quite some time. For the most part, the 2006 remake stays true to the original film exactly, simply revitalizing it with a modern take. And for once, it actually worked, so apparently the story is timeless, unlike many films which are so dated they can't be retold in another era accurately. Let's take a look at the actors. First billed was Julia Stiles. Like another reviewer, I felt that she was a bit too young to be playing the part of Damien's mother, since the actress in the original was a much older, more mature woman. However, she played the part well. Next, there was Liev Schrieber as Robert Thorn. Now, no offense, but that was quite a step down from the great Gregory Peck. Peck was a legend and an esteemed actor from the old days of Hollywood. But personally, I love Liev Schieber. I think he's been great in everything he's done and I thought he did an excellent job in this film. At times, his voice even sounded like Peck's, as did the photographer sound much like the English actor from the original. Next was Pete Postlethwaite as the priest who got speared. Another actor I love in every film I've seen him in, a terrific Shakespearean actor and a consummate professional. Believe it or not, I thought this part was a bit two dimensional and did not allow him to show the extent of his abilities, but for that simple reason, he did a great job with the role. Being someone who is tired with all the remakes today, and who has been generally disappointed with what Hollywood and the movie industry has become today, "The Omen" was a pleasant surprise. I thoroughly enjoyed this film, certainly more than I thought I would going into the theater. It stayed true to the original while adding a few new elements, some of which worked, others didn't, but neither altered or detracted from the film at all. It was serious enough, but didn't make the mistake of taking itself too seriously as so many movies do these days. It never felt over the top, even when considering the fact that it was indeed a big budget Hollywood production. The actors and their performances were on par, very believable and very effective. Overall, the entire production was very well done. My only gripes with the remake were firstly Marco Beltrami's score. The original, classic theme by Jerry Goldsmith as well as its entire influence on the movie were gone, replaced by another of Beltrami's bland works. I have never been impressed with his scores, from Scream to Resident Evil to Terminator 3, his music serves, but never paints a portrait, creates a mood, compliments the picture or captures the audience. In the case of The Omen, music was there when it needed to be, but nothing intriguing or remarkable and very forgettable. Secondly, this new movie followed the original so closely, it almost begged the question, "Why remake the film at all?" Why not simply leave the classic story allow. I think a friend of mine answered that best. This remake is a modern retelling for a new generation. It brings the story back for those who remember the original, never saw it, or were too young to understand it. And it does just that, and almost flawlessly if I might add. Now a whole generation who missed the original can experience this new film for themselves. That, and of course, the studio was able to capitalize of the significance of 666 and the release the date 6/6/06, which won't happen again for a thousand years. Again, not a perfect 10, but if you can watch it for what it is, a modern Hollywood remake, and accept it as such, I think you will enjoy this film. I did. Definitely a cut above the rest. If you liked the first Omen, go see this movie. 8 out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Silver Bullet</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Today's horror movies vs. horror movies 30 years ago</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After watching many horror remakes of some of my favorite classic horror movies (not to be confused with the 'old original' horror classics such as the original Dracula, Phantom of the Opera, etc) as well as horror movies made today and even the past 10 years, I've come to realize why many people are disappointed with today's horror/slasher movies (sans the remakes): It's the storytelling.The low-budget indie slasher flicks that have become cult favorites such as the Texas Chainsaw Massacre, Halloween, and even the original Friday the 13th, to name a few, while shot on extremely low budgets and poor effects that would seem amateur by today's standards did excel in one area and that was in its storytelling.Not just the plot, but the directing. The director's of these old slasher movies did a great job of drawing you into the story. Then there are the classic horror movies that stand out as incredible stories include the original Exorcist, Omen, and even the Prince of Darkness. These three movies I would categorize as horror/suspense whereas Texas Chainsaw Massacre, Friday the 13th, and similar movies would fall into the horror/slasher. There are several more to name off but the difference is the type of storytelling/directing.The movies of today and the past 10 years - most of them starting after the first Scream movie (which I still consider a horror/satire), are missing this art of storytelling. Even the horror/slasher films of today have very shallow plots. You can almost tell when a horror movie is going to have a shallow plot by the studio behind it and the excessive marketing (almost).Aside from the "Scream" carbon-copy movies in the late 90's, the movies in the past couple years such as Saw, Hostel, and See No Evil - while the latter is at least entertaining, they fail to draw the audience in with a great and original story.The realization came after watching See No Evil last week and The Omen remake tonight. The Omen remake maintains most of the style of the original, including the story element. It's hard to even categorize the both as horror movies because of the vast differences in storytelling.I suppose to conclude, I would have to say that it is the story or lack thereof that separates a horror/suspense from a horror/slasher movie.I liked all the movies I mentioned here, except Hostel which I found to be almost nothing but a studio promo. They hyped up the movie SO much and 'promised' some of the most shocking and horrifying cinema made yet, that when I finally saw it, I felt let down. Saving Private Ryan had more shockingly horrifying moments than Hostel.I also salute Rob Zombie for paying homage to the classic 70's horror/slasher genre yet maintaining originality in his storytelling, in his movie House of 1000 Corpses. This movie reminded me of a cross between Texas Chainsaw Massacre and 2000 Maniacs.I enjoyed the modernization of the Texas Chainsaw Massacre remake, however I've yet to see the 2000 Maniacs remake.The Omen remake did a great job of combining modern cinema-effects while preserving the art of the story. While some may consider the movie "slow", I attribute that to the A.D.D. that society has developed. I also enjoyed the modernization of this movie and the inclusion of footage of several recent catastrophic events used in the story. While I was a little surprised by this, I found it a clever way to use such tragic events respectfully, yet successfully in a horror movie.I give The Omen remake an 8 out of 10. And I plan to go see it again in the theater.-Silver Bullet P.S. Speaking of theaters, the opening night of The Omen, our theater's projector was running at a slightly slower speed causing the sound in the movie to be at a noticeably lower pitch. While this irritated me at first, I actually considered this may have been done on purpose on opening night (6/6/6) to add a creepy effect. I'm curious if anyone else in the country experienced the same effect?</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Brilliant!! Another hit horror remake!! Clever and intelligent!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It has been years since I've seen the original Omen series. It is a classic and it only made sense that Hollywood would remake the classic horror film. As of late I seem to be one of few that really, really enjoy these horror remakes. The brilliant The Hills Have Eyes, When A Stranger Calls and even last years The Fog was pretty well made. I have no issue with Hollywood creating a classic story for a new generation and even putting their own spin to it. Young director John Moore even goes out of his way to set the film up with some clever imagery, and hidden surprises for people. He doesn't tone the film down to keep in tough with the original but actually comes out of the gate all guns blazing.Liev Schreiber plays Robert Thorn, the Ambassador to Italy at the time of his wife's birth. In order to spare her pain he agrees to take a child that is not theirs and raise him as his own. I've seen Schreiber in a fair amount of things and he's always done okay. I've never really seen anything from him that would show me he could be a leading man in a film. Well The Omen did it!! He shows some real acting chops and showed that he could carry the film flawlessly. His intensity, his diplomacy in his political character really showed some maturity in his ability. He was great!! Julia Stiles plays Damien's mother Katherine Thorn. I like Stiles, I really do. She has done some great work in the Bourne films, 10 Things I Hate About You, and Save The Last Dance but I think she might have been miscast for this role. She doesn't come across as mature enough to be an Ambassador's wife and even when she begins to realize the truth about Damien she comes across as not being the good person. You don't feel sorry for her, and she just seems to be completely inferior to everyone else's character in the film. Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick is the infamous Damien, the child of Satan. Seamus is adequate. The story doesn't actually hinge on his performance but rather more the character and that's a good thing because he's not quite as creepy as the young child could be. I can think of many other child actors who could really send chills down your spine, although most of them are grown now. Still Seamus does a good enough job for the part and for being so young I'm sure he has a big career ahead of me. There were times he looked goofy more than scary. Pete Postlethwaite plays Father Brennan, and although his part is small he performance is quite significance. He has great screen presence and always adds something interesting to his character roles. He meets a brutal ending in the film. Mia Farrow is deliciously evil as the new nanny Mrs. Baylock, determined to protect Damien against anything or anyone including his own parents. Her soft spoken voice but glaring evil eyes make her a terrific villain and the battle between her and Liev Schrieber at the end is edge of your seat terrifying.It really isn't the characters that truly make this film really, really well done but rather the story and the equal balance between gore horror and psychological horror. The film is absolutely gory with some truly unique, and disgusting kills but it also adds in that creepy, skin crawling aspect of Julia Stiles' dreams, and visions. The film is intelligent enough to have certain aspects of imagery such as the deep red on white background shown repeatedly and the certain flashes of 6.6.6. The setting for this film is brilliant, from Italy, to England, to Israel...the architecture for every building no matter how mundane is breath taking. Each scene is dark, gritty, ancient, beautifully shot. Schrieber absolutely holds together the story and is definitely watchable. If you loved the original, if you love a good horror/thriller you will thoroughly enjoy The Omen. On top of it being gory, captivating, thrilling they even have plenty of what I always call "cheap scares" where you will literally jump in the air at the sound of something jumping out, crashing down, being where it shouldn't be...always a lot of fun and The Omen is worth seeing. 9/10</fullReview>

		<date>2006-06-07</date>
		<username>Lee Alon</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A Portent of Good Things to Come</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Probably the only aspect of this remake altered significantly from the original motion picture was the international release date, specifically chosen by the studio to play on number of the beast associations. Thirty years ago it the world was different, with no such thing as a universal release (The Omen 2006 came out everywhere on the same fateful day), and perhaps a mite more sensitivity to issues satanic, hence the caution evident back then.But aside from release dates as marketing ploys, scant has changed. Characters and events follow the 1976 edition almost to a tee, and even names haven't been tampered with as often happens with remakes. Some might claim this indicates a lack of innovation, since connecting the dots doesn't entirely amount to pristine movie making at its pinnacle. After all, three decades are quite the interim, and there was much producers could have done to keep current aside from incorporating cell phones and LCD monitors.This reviewer begs to differ. Young director John Moore, who previously made only a handful of movies like Behind Enemy Lines and Flight of the Phoenix, has steered this release safely onto the calm waters of distinct flavor, helping bestow on the newer picture a mood peculiar to it, delineating the tendency to attempt and recapture the vibe of an older release (a venture frequently doomed to failure), and erecting a structure able to withstand the vagaries of a summer season bristling with high-profile remakes (see Miami Vice).So while similarities essentially amount to story ingredients, 2006 sees a drastically different movie than the one shown audiences back in the 70's. Additionally, because the story's so well known and established, there's very little to surprise viewers in that regard. Ergo, Moore's decision to focus on stylistic modification paid off, with The Omen coming across a compelling watch despite harboring no major twists, and being shown to people the world over who've been jaded clean by scores of conspiracy stories packing much more shock value than The Omen franchise. Having the movie still end up enjoyable in light of all this is testament to its success.Likely the biggest difference one notes upon seeing the remake is the bigger deal they make of the whole 666 thing, although it was also present in the 1976 iteration to some extent. Here it's seen as a birthmark denoting Damien Thorn (Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick) as a hell-spawn little brat unknowingly visited on aristocrats the Thorns, a family of well-to-do upper-crustaceans with strong ties to the American political machine.Father Robert (Liev Schreiber) chooses to feign ignorance for as long as possible as a benefit to his fragile wife Katherine (Julia Stiles). The illusion comes to a grinding halt when signs of imminent Armageddon are observed by Vatican officials, prompting an attempt to locate Damien and pre-empt evil's amassed onslaught on humanity. One clergyman, Father Brennan (Pete Postelthwaite) tries harder than most to contact the family and warn them, but meets with yet another grisly horror-movie demise.Another story-arc revolves around Robert joining forces with intrepid paparazzi reporter Keith Jennings (David Thewlis from Naked and Kingdom of Heaven), hoping to identify the true nature of Damien's evil and his origins as the son of Satan. This takes them to various locations in Italy and the Middle East, of course putting them in harm's way.It's funny so much of the story can be given away without risking overexposure and spoilage, but then it's a much-retold horror standard and most people are familiar with it one way or another. The Omen 2006 bypasses this handicap when it provides solid entertainment comprising manifold, diverse parts while never becoming bogged down or even near the point of boredom. It's evident careful consideration was allotted each segment, and the movie moves along effortlessly. It's also OK to look at artistically, featuring gore in keeping with its genre, but also convincing locales and effects.Acting's not as tight as might have been hoped for, particularly the interaction between Schreiber and the oft excellently-detached Stiles felt too mechanical and forced, while young Davey-Fitzpatrick's cover of Damien seemed perfunctorily economical, imbuing no real sensation either way. We knew what he was all about by virtue of the well-traversed story, not more.On the other hand, Thewlis and Schreiber communicated beautifully, giving off a very workable relationship, making us believe they really were working against the clock and had something common of great value at stake here. Another highlight came from Mia Farrow, rendering wicked Nanny Baylock (before done by Billie Whitelaw) as the conduit by which evil's maintained a safe haven in the Thorn household. It's good to see the veteran actress in the limelight again, although few performances can come close to dizzying heights she's achieved with Rosemary's Baby, arguably one of the classics that inspired the look and feel of 1976 The Omen.This incarnation also ends obviously on a sequelish note, so expect remakes of several other Omen story lines in the near future. Put together with other points made above, this qualified Omen '06 as a competent work of interpretive cinema and an affable summer treat. It has more to do with gore than horror, and seasoned viewers have nothing to fear within. Mainstream or lay audiences were probably more of a target for 20th Century Fox, providing screams and gasps as the movie dishes out one of several ghoulish moments courtesy of hell hounds, beheadings and implements of impalement.A good reprise, The Omen returns to satisfy with the series' trademark wink. Not to be taken too seriously, it opens up possibilities for resurgent enfranchisement, and hope for horror-leaning movie buffs everywhere.Rating: * * * *</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>nobbytatoes</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>a return to old fashion horror</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Vatican has just interpreted the last sign of the impending apocalypse; a comet falling from the sky. On this night, the son of the anti-Christ is born. In Rome, Robert Thorn's wife Katherine has just given birth. During the delivery complication arose and unbeknown to Katherine that she lost the baby. In the same hour another baby was born, with the mother dying during the delivery. Robert takes this baby and raises it as his own; keeping the paternity from Katherine. Five years later, Robert is ambassador for Britain and the family is at its happiest. At Damien's fifth birthday party, the current nanny hangs herself from the roof; screaming her death is for Damien. Soon strange things start happening around Damien. Robert is approached by Father Brennan, claiming that Robert's son is the anti-Christ and has to be killed. Robert doesn't believe this to be true, though as time passes and the strange coincidences stack up, Robert believes that he and Katherine are in danger from their son.Once again, the screens are graced by yet another remake of a classic horror film. Though the pressing question is does this remake fall into the pit of despair that so many of the other remakes have fallen in to? Surprisingly The Omen just survives this fate. With all the current disaster's of recent time; 9/11, earthquakes, floods, tsunamis, etc, writer David Seltzer has cleverly used these as a means of the plots device claiming the Apocalypse is nigh; especially on it's release date 6/6/06; coincidence or clever marketing.This version of The Omen is a trek back to basic horror, implementing mood, tension and foreboding atmosphere. The recent trend of many horror films has been on the amount of violence you can through at your audience, so it's refreshing and gratifying that director John Moore has returned to this style. Disappointing this tension isn't kept at the same level for the entirety of the film. The beginning is choppy with some awkward directing and editing, which lessening the first lot of scares. The Omen takes it's time to build, trying to find its footings, but really comes together mid point onwards.The amount of violence and noise scares are used quite minimally, though very effective when used. What made the original Omen so interesting is that director Richard Donner took away the safety net of the three second look away. He found that during the scare, people would look away for three seconds, to find the dead body to be gone when they look back at the screen. Brilliantly Richard extended the scare time, so when people's eyes returned to the screen, they were surprised to see the decapitated head still rolling in the air. John Moore has taken this approach in this version, but it's not as effective, though it's pleasant that he takes a subtle approach to the violence, instead of throwing it everywhere.Liev Schreiber is strong as Robert Thorn, yet doesn't have that commanding presence as Gregory Peck. Julia Stiles didn't seem that convincing as Katherine, with a sub standard performance. Pete Postlethwaite comes off pedantic as Father Brennan. David Thewlis is strong, keeping his role simple and not over acting. The real surprise is from Mia Farrow. It's such a delight watching her; since she knows what it's like to raise the son of the devil.The Omen has it's problems and flaws, but it's so much better than a lot of the other horror remakes.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>THE OMEN (2006) *** Liev Schreiber, Julia Stiles, Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick, Mia Farrow, David Thewlis, Pete Postlethwaite, Michael Gambon, Amy Huck, Giovanni Lombardo Radice. Better-than-anticipated remake of the cult occult horror classic about the birth of the Anti-Christ, Damien Thorn (creepy little Davey-Fitzpatrick sportin' a mean scowl and a neo-Moe Howard bowl cut; talk about creepy!), unbeknownst to his loving parents (Schreiber who teeters- towards-irritated brow-kneading until the final act and delivers the anti-hero goods and a wan Stiles), an American diplomat and his wife in Great Britain. More-or-less an amped up sequence-by-sequence reshoot largely due to original film's scribe David Seltzer (adding a few goose-bumps here and there) and director John Moore's lavish production design making up for the all-too familiar for those who've seen it; for those experiencing the ultimate evil consider it in the vein of "Final Destination" meets The Devil. Some jolts thanks to the razor-sharp editing by Dan Zimmerman in spite of the headache-inducing score by Marco Beltrami. Nice touch of red sprinkled crimson throughout by production designer Patrick Lumb and stunt-casting of Farrow - the real mother of another Satanic off-spring in "Rosemary's Baby" - as the sweetly sinister nanny from Hell makes things lively giddy.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Critics usually complain about a remake if it's too different from the original or if it's too similar. No one can complain about the script, because the same person that wrote the original wrote this one, which is a plus. And director John Moore left nothing to be questioned, left no doubts as to how someone got here or there or why they would be there in the first place. Instead, it makes you genuinely feel for this family, and the acting (mostly Liev Schreiber's) makes the story feel as realistic as it can possibly be.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Horror mixed with nostalgia?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>FIRST IMPRESSIONS:I saw the original Omen when I was about 10. Being over 30, I still remember it!!! The funny thing is that this remake made me suffer? I was even under the grip of it all the way! OK, I get scared with horror movies. And I did my "homework": watched it alone, on a foggy BA night, sat at the loneliest place of the cinema and went in an awkward time so that NOBODY would spoil me the fun of ? getting scared to death :)!!Let's start by the beginning: this is the best "religious horror" film I've ever seen! I also liked Stigmata (knowing it was far more commercial) and the famous "The exorcist", which even in its revamped version didn't get much of me. Exorcist: The Beginning was OK but... just incomparable to this MASTERPIECE. The Omen is as central to this genre of films as? The Godfather to films in general!!ACTING:Lieb Schreiber is a great actor, he doesn't overdo any single scene; the film is basically carried by him. Visually, there's an air of his directorial debut "Everything is illuminated" (2005) when they tour Italy in search of the truth, but the tone is definitely more sombre! Julia Stiles has the kind of sexless, "noseless" and elegant beauty becoming for a diplomat of high rank (and a president's daughter, to boot!). My favourite is Mia FARROW as the unpretentious Mrs. Baylock. How not to be taken in by such motherly love and selfless duty? OK, her character could be a bit more developed, but she is genuinely FEARSOME? I think I'm gonna have nightmares with her as a nanny : )!"Amy Huck", their first nanny, is a hidden gem of "character composition". Just two sentences and one lost regard let us anticipate? The casting is perfect (Susie Figgis)! And the unlikely director of this orchestra is Moore.I think that the original Damien, "Harvey Stephens" has a meaner look in his green eyes that makes him far better than his replacement. More nuanced, "British", and perverse. I've just read on IMDb that he had a small role in this sequel, cool!"Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick"? I could even agree with the extremist view of MICHAEL ACUNA (Glendale, California): "woefully miscast anti-Christ Damien of Fitzpatrick. And when the child of the Devil is boring in a film about itself, what have we got?" Also agree with Culbertson from Osaka: "looks more like he is spoiled than scary".OTHER REVIEWERS: Amazon's Jeff Shannon says it better than me: "At a time when graphic gore had yet to dominate the horror genre, this film used its violence discreetly and to great effect, and the mood of dread and potential death is masterfully maintained" (he writes about the original version). IMDb reviewer David Duncan from Australia says that it lacks? "sense of foreboding". Not only I DO find it, I think it's the key of the film! NOT "the scares", specially the effective nightmares. For that there are many more "specialized" films, most of them nameless gore alla Tarantino. MY TAKE:On a personal level, this film meant a lot to me. Maybe because I looked alike the original Damien (1976 version) when I was a child. So it was told by my cousins! I haven't checked under my hair for "666" birthmarks :)!I feel there must be something in that well told tale of Catholicism that makes ground for such creepy horror films, but I won't explore that idea for many reasons!!The pivotal moment is in Italy, when Robert screams "There's nothing, only here, and life" and then?How could we even compare all the current manufactured "blood spitting films" with this deeply moving human account of a man who had it all, and then it had nothing less that the most gruesome task ahead? Maybe it's a generational thing, I felt horror physically when I saw the original movie (kids were much more innocent in the mid 80s). And 15 years is enough time to have forgotten basically all about the film (hey, I didn't even remembered how it ended!).But, somehow, the creepy eerie foreboding remains. Concretely, when I saw the beginning of the "big scenes", I couldn't help but recognize them. Even probably know how they would scare me? and I enjoyed every moment of it!!Many scenes are classics, and music (M. Beltrami) does its job very well. I'll never see this kind of flat bikes children use without remorse, the same goes for rottweilers and black dogs in general. And I wouldn't mind not stepping into churches or getting in contact with priests for a while either :) !The scene at the cemetery exposes ourselves with our basic fear of death; the one of the zoo, fear of beasts (but the original was better); the party in the beginning, how bad can things get in a public place. (I don't incur into SPOILERS, like many others reviewers do? (ie F. Nores in the original)). Cinematography (J. Sela) and editing are what makes this film stand the test of time. Details are of the essence: I'll just mention the warning skull when Robert enters the hospital, and the bathroom scene with Katherine looking like, yes, Christ over a minimalist cross. Speaking of minimalism, the shrink's consulting room is church-like and dreamy! I want to do therapy with this room :)!CONCLUSION: I LOVED this film, but understand that other people may watch this "rationally" and feel it's below par. How many films of the hundreds one watches stay in our memory for 15 years? This one worked for me! Were 1/5 of the films I watch so moving, I would try to spend the rest of my life watching films and writing reviews like this one, listening to baroque music of course :) !</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Excellent remake!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>So I briefly browsed through the postings for The Omen on the IMDb and noticed that someone had started a line of postings about strange occurrences on 6/6/06. I have one for you, although mine is more disappointing than strange. I go to the Marina Cinema in Marina del Rey, right by where I live, and I arrive minutes before screen time, walk around the corner and find a massive line for the ticket booth. Seconds from giving up and going home, I decide to stay in line because the whole reason I came to the movies on a Tuesday by myself after work was because I wanted the ticket stub for The Omen dated 6/6/06, so I stay in line. The line goes fast enough so that I get through in time enough even to stop and buy popcorn and a coke, the dietary supplement that I am by now convinced will eventually kill me, and walk into the theater. The lady tears my ticket, says "The Omen, Theater 2," and I'm on my way. I thoroughly enjoy the movie, and as I leave the theater with a smile on my face, I take my ticket out of my pocket, having not even looked at it since I purchased it, and as I look down to see how prominent the movie title and the date are displayed, I stop in shock as I read:Marina Unit 2418PresentingBREAK-UP-THE4:10PM TUE 6/6/06Aud #1I stop in my tracks in dismay, unfortunately in the middle of the street, and get honked at by the passing car that almost hits me. At least they got the date right?Getting to the movie, I was surprised at how good it was, because it could so easily have been watered down, or have had the satanic element wildly exaggerated to appease the goofy teenage goth crowd that surrounded and horrendously irritated me throughout the film (I was lucky enough to sit right in front of the one jerkoff who burst out into hysterical laughter every time Damien was on screen, an excellent way to summarily ruin the movie). Live Schreiber gives an outstanding performance as Robert Thorn, an up and coming young American Ambassador, surpassed perhaps only by Julia Stiles, whose portrayal of his increasingly disturbed wife is at once maternally comforting, subsequently unsettling and astonishingly accurate throughout. Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick delivers an incredible performance as the satanic title character. In some scenes he is nothing short of frightening, such as when he is making a sandwich in the middle of the night and stares motionless at his awoken and inquisitive mother. At other times, however, the grimaces that he gives bring to my mind the image of the director behind the camera telling him to make a real, real mean face. Then again, that impression could very well have been inspired by that idiot behind me, laughing in my ear. I found some of the film to be a bit talky and slow, but it maintains a creepiness throughout and has some outstanding shocks, which is one of the areas that a movie like this is most likely to screw up, because the audience walks into the auditorium expecting and watching for them, and it is so hard to come up with something original. Remember the girl getting hit by the bus in Final Destination? That is an example of originality. If there was a screeching cat in this movie it would have been a struggle for me to resist walking out of the theater, but the scares were plenty and they were effective. I have to say that I found it a bit odd to see the Italian priest at the beginning of the film giving a lecture about what he believed signaled the coming of the Antichrist, since it was inter cut with a lot of American tragedies. But no matter, the film was well made, the performances were spot on, and it was faithful to the original without being a simple re- shooting, as was famously the case with the Psycho re-make.. It's easy to immediately condemn a re-make of any kind as teetering on the verge of insulting the integrity and sanctity of the original, but given the sheer onslaught of remakes that are pouring out of Hollywood these days, I find myself pretty thankful to see one as faithful, yet original, as this one.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>The Omen (2006) is a horror film that stands out among the rest. It's not perfect - it's slow in points, sometimes laughable, and Julia Stiles is not the best actress.I've seen the original, but it was such a long time ago that all I remember are the violent scenes. I don't remember particularly liking it, in fact, I may have fallen asleep (this was around 3 a.m., however).Before this movie and certain others I've watched recently I despised "jump" scares. I thought they required no skill in scaring the viewer. But in fact they can be used very effectively, it's just that most horror movies perform them miserably. They're used to give you a taste of the shock that the character is going through. It works in The Omen because it has a good, involving story. You get sucked in, thus the startling scenes are all the more effective. This movie has by far the two best jump scares I've ever seen. I almost never fall for them - I've been startled a couple times, but in this movie I actually felt terror. I literally jumped in my seat and yelled. I haven't had that good of an adrenaline rush in a long time - very satisfying. In another scene involving a bathroom mirror the theater literally shook with all the people leaping in their chairs and shrieking.The things that made this movie scary are these: -The use of nightmares. The only other movies I can think of that realistically portray nightmares are The Exorcist, Eternal Sunshine of the Spotless Mind, and Jacob's Ladder.-Restraint. One scene takes place in a zoo. The apes go bananas when they see Damien, pounding the glass until it cracks. Schoolchildren run and scream in fear. The scene ends, leaving you to imagine what hell would've broken loose had the apes escaped.-I've always thought hangings are disturbing. There's a good one in here.-Freak accidents ala Final Destination. These are scary because they happen in real life. The idea that something intangible is at work trying to kill you and there is no escape is unsettling. In one scene a man is decapitated and his headless corpse is left standing for a few seconds before tumbling headless over heels down a set of stairs. These scenes are even more shocking when they happen in broad daylight in front of large groups of people (perhaps a football arena that catches fire?) Plus the cinematography is good. I can't believe this only has a 5.3 on IMDb and also bad reviews on rotten tomatoes. See it wanting to have a good time and get scared.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Entertaining mix of modern and old-fashioned movie-making</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was invited by Fox Germany to a preview in a cinema in Essen last night, in connection with the annual meeting of the German skeptics society GWUP (some members of which were also included in a panel discussion after the screening). "The Omen" is a remake of the 1976 classic, following practically the same script but with certain updates in details. E.g. major news events of the past few years have been integrated into the pretty gripping opening scenes in 'the Vatican' as evidence for the upcoming apocalypse, and characters, esp. the female lead, have been developed forward into the 21st century. The overall feel of the movie, however, is much like your typical religious horror flick of decades past, with lots of rain, horror effects that are sometimes stark and at times just funny, and a very dark and unreal atmosphere. I must say that I didn't expect much but was positively surprised - my favorite 'paranormal' movie is still 'The Medusa Touch', but this one is well worth your attention. It also provoked me to do some detective work on the location where the very first scene (with a 'Vatican astronomer' at an old-fashioned telescope) was shot: Since the press material said that nearly everything was filmed in or around Prague, I checked out pictures of the astronomical institutions there and found the double refractor of Stefanik Observatory above the city a perfect match ...</fullReview>

The casting of Julia Stiles as the wife is just idiotic. Her performance rings false whenever she's supposed to emote, and she, like Liev, comes off as a bore during most of their scenes. Pete Postlethwaite and Michael Gambon ham it up as the wacky priests who are hard to take seriously, while Mia Farrow overacts like hell as the evil nurse. As for Damien himself, well, let's hope Seamus Davey-Fitzpatrick never gets another role. He's absolutely awful and his forced attempts at being creepy are just ridiculous. If you think I'm being too hard on the kid, I suggest you check out movies like The Sixth Sense, The Shining, or, hell, even the original "Omen". Now THAT'S good child acting! Moving on: when the hell did "horror" become synonymous with "things that jump at you from behind"? Seriously, this sort of thing isn't scary. Anyone can film a vaguely frightening object, add a deafening noise and cut away quickly. It's not exactly the sort of thing that you'll lose sleep over. I mean, if you've seen that "creepy car commercial", then you've seen this. A very weak film with no atmosphere to speak of, and the fact that it is a remake of one of my favourite films of all time only makes it worse.</fullReview>
	</review>

When this was released it was not just the 30th Anniversary of the original, but it was out on the 6th of June (6th month) of 2006! Anyway, the story has not made any changes, you still have Robert Thorn (Scream's Liev Schreiber) taking a lone baby, after his own dies. This kid grows into Damien (Seamus Davey-Kirkpatrick), son of the devil, and many bizarre things happen around and during his time, including the hanging babysitter, the protection evil Mrs. Baylock (Rosemary's Baby's Mia Farrow), deadly dogs, raging monkeys (now gorillas behind glass), the death of crazy Father Brennan (Brassed Off's Pete Postlethwaite), and of course, the famous beheading scene for Jennings (Harry Potter's David Thewlis). There are moments where it can be creepy, including the good opening of distinctive moments mentioned in the bible coming true, and using real footage, but ultimately, it is just another typical "not as good as the original" remake. Also starring a brief Sir Michael Gambon as Bugenhagen. Worth watching!</fullReview>
